2014</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</section>\n<section class="fullwidth article">\n<div class="container">\n<div class="content-border">\n<div class="tab">\n                        WEEK 4:\n                    </div>\n<div class="share" style="padding-bottom:20px;">\n<div class="a2a_kit a2a_kit_size_32 a2a_default_style" data-a2a-title="Step-by-step plan to lift barriers to SME finance" data-a2a-url="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/">\n<a class="a2a_button_facebook"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_twitter"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_google_plus"></a>\n<a class="a2a_dd" href="https://www.addtoany.com/share"></a>\n</div>\n<script async="" src="//static.addtoany.com/menu/page.js"></script>\n</div>\n<h3 class="tracked_title tracked_port" style="padding-top:20px;">Step-by-step plan to lift barriers to SME finance</h3>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-sm-8">\n<div class="main-content">\n<div class="content-pic">\n<p></p>\n<p></p>\n</div>\n<div class="content" id="main-content">\n<p><strong>In the last of the series, Phil Thornton looks at the EU\xe2\x80\x99s initiative to achieve cross-border capital markets union.</strong></p>\n<p>Small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) are the backbone of Europe\xe2\x80\x99s economy, providing 85 per cent of all new jobs.  Since the global financial crisis, however, European businesses and especially SMEs have been caught in a cruel catch-22 trap.</p>\n<p>Traditionally more reliant on bank loans than market-based finance compared with their American counterparts, many companies have struggled to access finance as the banks sought to restore their balance sheets to meet tighter regulations brought in following the financial crisis.</p>\n<p>Brussels is now looking to bridge the gap with a series of initiatives designed to kickstart lending to SMEs.  It\xe2\x80\x99s Competitiveness of Enterprises and Small and Medium-sized Enterprises (COSME) initiative has a budget of over \xe2\x82\xac1.3billion (\xc2\xa3940 million) to fund these financial instruments that facilitate access to loans and equity finance for SMEs where market gaps have been identified.</p>\n<p>Part of the COSME budget, which runs until 2020, will fund guarantees and counter-guarantees for financial intermediaries, such as banks and leasing companies, to help them provide more loan and lease finance to SMEs.  Its most significant intervention was the unveiling of an action plan in September this year to help build a true capital markets union (CMU) across all 28 EU states.  The key elements are:</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\xa2\tLegislation to establish a framework for simple, transparent and standardised securitisation</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\xa2\tChanges to the Solvency II rules to make it easier for insurers to invest in infrastructure and European Long Term Investment Funds</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\xa2\tConsultation on whether changes to the venture capital regulations could boost the take-up of these funds</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\xa2\tReview of the Prospectus Directive to reduce barriers to smaller firms listing on markets</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\xa2\tGreen Paper on retail financial services to boost consumer choice and competition in cross border retail financial services and insurance</p>\n<p>Lord (Jonathan) Hill, the EU Commissioner whose remit includes capital markets union, said in October that CMU was essential to deliver deeper capital markets to complement bank lending and support European growth. \xe2\x80\x9cWe will look at how to remove barriers to small firms raising money from capital markets, and how we can better connect information on investment opportunities in SMEs to investors the world over,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.</p>\n<p>Thanks to banking union, European banks have been able to invest cross border, but individuals and small businesses have found national legislation means it is hard to move money between different countries.</p>\n<p>Christian Odendahl, chief economist at the Centre for European Reform, gives the example of an investor in London looking to put money in a Czech start-up finding issues such as the prospectus document, tax treatment and insolvency regime are all matters of national competence.</p>\n<p>It is exactly these issues that have proved the hardest nuts to crack.  Graham Bishop, a former City economist and consultant on EU integration, said: \xe2\x80\x9cOther commissioners have already picked the low hanging fruit, so Commissioner Hill has been left with the nettles to grasp.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p>He welcomed the move to consult on the various insolvency and restructuring regimes across the EU and come forward with a legislative plan by 2016.  \xe2\x80\x9cThey have to do this because if they don\xe2\x80\x99t, they\xe2\x80\x99re not serious.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p>Odendahl agreed. \xe2\x80\x9cMost of the steps they plan to take make a lot of sense, such as the modernisation of the prospectus directive to help smaller businesses take part in capital markets, or to revise the regulatory treatment of infrastructure investment,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.</p>\n<p>Hill faced two choices.  Once was to mimic the strategy with banking union when the Commission put the powerful institution of the European Central Bank in charge.  However, that would have involved driving through harmonisation of national regulation at a time when member states were struggling to deal with the refugee crisis and negotiations ahead of the UK referendum on EU membership.</p>\n<p>The alternative was to take a step-by-step approach to tackling the individual barriers. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are a lot of constraints holding back a properly unified capital market,\xe2\x80\x9d Odendahl said.</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\x9cHill has looked at what are the most binding constraints on cross-border capital flows, and where Europe can achieve the most bang for the buck, given that banking union-type full integration is not on the cards.</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\x9cThe question is how much we can achieve with small steps in terms of convergence in how we treat capital market products and make sure investors in, say, London have enough confidence in a capital market of another country.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p>If the process leads to simpler and better regulation, it could benefit supporters of a yes vote in the referendum. \xe2\x80\x9cPeople who understand capital markets will see that as a good thing,\xe2\x80\x9d Odendahl said.  \xe2\x80\x9cBeing successful on an issue like capital markets union is a positive for the \xe2\x80\x98In\xe2\x80\x99 campaign.  It is certainly not something that the \xe2\x80\x98out\xe2\x80\x99 campaign will point to as a bad thing for Britain.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n</div>\n<div class="quote hidden-xs">\n<img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xquotes.jpg.pagespeed.ic.0Ijl46bHM-.jpg"/>\n<p>If the process leads to simpler and better regulation, it could benefit supporters of a yes vote in the referendum.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-4">\n<div class="bg-blue gt-article">\n<h5 style="margin:0;">Advertisement Feature</h5>\n<div class="gt-title">\n<h3>Comment &amp; Analysis</h3>\n<p>Jim Esposito</p>\n<p>Global Co-Head of Financing Group</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/xJim-Esposito.jpg.pagespeed.ic.KwHHMrt-DI.jpg"/>\n<p><strong>Goldman Sachs</strong></p>\n</div>\n<div class="gt-linebreak"></div>\n<p>Free markets bring all-round benefits<br/>\n<br/>\r\nToday\xe2\x80\x99s institutional capital markets play a central role in allocating funds between investors who provide capital and companies who consume capital.  The more efficient and free these markets, the greater the benefits to each side. Greater efficiency means cheaper capital for companies to grow and a broader more diversified array of assets for investors to deploy their capital.<br/>\n<br/>\r\nGreater efficiency is driven by greater competition, and companies benefit hugely from being able to choose between accessing the bond and institutional loan markets or their traditional bank.  Securitisation can boost traditional lending by enabling new challenger banks and speciality finance platforms, and by providing an additional source of funding and risk sharing to traditional banks.<br/>\n<br/>\r\nSubstantial reforms have been made to the financial services industry globally, with banks becoming more strongly capitalised.  These reforms have impacted credit supply to European borrowers from traditional banks.  Institutional capital markets are able to not only replace this source of credit, but they can often represent cheaper options, with fewer restrictions.<br/>\n<br/>\r\nGiven the recent maturation of the European debt capital markets, companies are increasingly confident that they can complement the traditional banking relationships by accessing the institutional capital markets.<br/>\n<br/>\r\nPotentially the most important users of capital in Europe today are those companies that need to invest in growth. Their success can be enhanced by access to diversified sources of capital beyond the traditional commercial banks.  Many of these companies, however, are rated below investment grade given their size.  Changes in regulation could unlock incremental capital for these companies. Regulatory changes that provide greater freedom to investors, combined with changes to legal frameworks that deal with bankruptcy and insolvency, would help companies and investors alike.<br/>\n<br/>\r\nTake infrastructure, a sector that provides benefits to towns, cities and national economies.  The increased focus on levels of public debt across Europe meant that investment in infrastructure has suffered.  This is often considered one reason for the reduced long term growth rate.  Broader institutional investment can provide an effective solution by supplementing both public institutions and traditional commercial banking in this critical sector.</p>\n<p class="gt-terms">This content was produced by the Advertising Department of The Times in collaboration with Goldman Sachs. The news and editorial staffs of The Times had no role in its preparation.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<!-- end of the loop -->\n<!-- pagination here -->\n</div>\n</div>\n</section>\n<section class="fullwidth related-media">\n<div class="tracked_article bg-white" id="innovative-ways-to-fund-businesses-are-on-the-increase-and-are-opening-new-doors-for-smes">\n<div class="container">\n<div class="content-border">\n<div class="tab">\n                                        MORE FROM THE TIMES\n                                    </div>\n<h3>Innovative ways to fund businesses are on the increase and are opening new doors for SMEs</h3>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="related-articles">\n<div class="col-sm-10 col-sm-offset-1">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="sub-article-excerpt">\n<div class="col-sm-7">\n<img alt="Euro_650x300" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" data-lazy-src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/10/Euro_650x300.jpg" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/lazy-load/images/1x1.trans.gif"/><noscript><img alt="Euro_650x300" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/10/xEuro_650x300.jpg.pagespeed.ic.JVIzacIhZp.jpg"/></noscript> </div>\n<div class="col-sm-5">\n<p><strong>Alternative finance is often equated with peer-to-peer lending and other types of crowdfunding but there is more to it than that, according to Bryan Zhang, a director of Judge Business School\xe2\x80\x99s Cambridge Centre for Alternative Finance.</strong> \xe2\x80\x9cIt is any form of financial channels and instruments that have emerged outside the traditional financial system,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. This does include crowdfunding in all its forms (see last\xe2\x80\xa6</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="sub-article-content">\n<div class="col-sm-12">\n<img alt="Euro_650x300" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" data-lazy-src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/10/Euro_650x300.jpg" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/lazy-load/images/1x1.trans.gif"/><noscript><img alt="Euro_650x300" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/10/xEuro_650x300.jpg.pagespeed.ic.JVIzacIhZp.jpg"/></noscript> <p><p><strong>Alternative finance is often equated with peer-to-peer lending and other types of crowdfunding but there is more to it than that, according to Bryan Zhang, a director of Judge Business School\xe2\x80\x99s Cambridge Centre for Alternative Finance.</strong> \xe2\x80\x9cIt is any form of financial channels and instruments that have emerged outside the traditional financial system,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. This does include crowdfunding in all its forms (see last week\xe2\x80\x99s article) but it also covers alternative currencies, such as Bitcoin; online currency exchange platforms; pension-led business funding; online invoice trading; and mini-bonds. Each of the last three can be used for business funding, either to improve cash flow or to access capital for growth.</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\x9cInvoice trading and pension-led funding are much less well-known in comparison to peer-to-peer lending,\xe2\x80\x9d said Zhang. \xe2\x80\x9cThese are not more complex or more risky, they are just newer, and there is a smaller market than for other types of alternative finance.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p>Invoice trading is the best known of these newer tools, probably because banks have offered a similar service for quite some time, said Zhang. The idea is that SMEs sell their unpaid invoices to investors at a discount, meaning that they get their money much faster than waiting for their clients to pay up, improving the business\xe2\x80\x99s cash flow and reducing the amount of time that they need to spend chasing late payers. It is an approach that can prove particularly useful for consultancies and other businesses that sell services rather than products because it is not a loan, and therefore does not require the business to put up stock or property as collateral.</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\x9cQuite a few (start-ups) use invoice trading because the banks do not get their business model,\xe2\x80\x9d said Zhang. \xe2\x80\x9cOften these are from the knowledge economy \xe2\x80\x93 consultancies, say \xe2\x80\x93 and do not fit the standard borrowing profile. So sometimes banks do not get it but platforms like Market Invoice are fintech companies, and they get it. They know how to evaluate the risk because they have a better understanding of the business model.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nOnline invoice trading platforms also offer a service that is cheaper and significantly more flexible than that offered by banks, he added. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you go to a bank that offers invoice factoring it could take days, weeks or even months (to get your money) but online it would be minutes, or maybe hours.\xe2\x80\x9d It also gives the SME more control, as online platforms let sellers decide which invoices they want to offer up; banks, on the other hand, usually want all or nothing.</p>\n<p>Pension-led funding, meanwhile, allows the owners or directors of an SME to tap into a pension fund \xe2\x80\x93 often one accrued while working in a more traditional corporate job before turning entrepreneur \xe2\x80\x93 through a SIPP to invest their money into their own business, said Zhang. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s liberating their pension pot to use for working capital or expansion capital.\xe2\x80\x9d It is not risk-free; it is also bound by the strict regulations that already govern pensions, he added.</p>\n<p>One alternative finance growth area where regulation is still on the vague side is mini-bonds \xe2\x80\x93 ones issued directly by small companies. \xe2\x80\x9cUsually bonds are very much a corporate thing with some sort of rating behind them as a lending instrument offered to the public,\xe2\x80\x9d said Norbert Morawetz, a lecturer in entrepreneurship at Henley Business School. \xe2\x80\x9cWith mini-bonds, companies offer structured loans (directly) to the public. In other words they are disintermediating.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p>For investors and SMEs alike it is a market that \xe2\x80\x9crequires a bit more savvy\xe2\x80\x9d but gives the borrower more flexibility that is possible using a peer-lending platform. \xe2\x80\x9cWith bonds (the borrower) has more control and can set their own rate and offer longer-term premiums than a platform would. And typically with bonds you can also trade them and sell them on, although I have not seen that yet. That (would) create a different alternative finance market to trade these bonds on,\xe2\x80\x9d he added.</p>\n<p>Morawetz is curious to see whether such a secondary market will emerge \xe2\x80\x93 and indeed to find out which directions fintechs will take alternative finance next. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you look at alternative finance now, it has replicated and innovated on what traditional banking used to do,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThey have made it more efficient in some ways, for instance with the use of algorithms and big data. Beyond that it will be interesting to see what the new innovations will be. In some ways the big opportunities, the easy targets, have been addressed.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="share">\n<div class="a2a_kit a2a_kit_size_32 a2a_default_style" data-a2a-title="Innovative ways to fund businesses are on the increase and are opening new doors for SMEs" data-a2a-url="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/week4/innovative-ways-to-fund-businesses-are-on-the-increase-and-are-opening-new-doors-for-smes/">\n<a class="a2a_button_facebook"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_twitter"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_google_plus"></a>\n<a class="a2a_dd" href="https://www.addtoany.com/share"></a>\n</div>\n<script async="" src="//static.addtoany.com/menu/page.js"></script>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p style="color:#000;font-size:14px;margin-top:15px;"></p>\n</div>\n<button class="btn readmore">Read More</button>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="bg-blue" id="the-potential-benefits-of-cmu">\n<div class="container">\n<div class="content-border">\n<div class="tab">\n                                        VIDEO\n                                    </div>\n<h3 class="tracked_port">The potential benefits of CMU</h3>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="related-articles">\n<div class="col-sm-10 col-sm-offset-1">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="sub-article-excerpt">\n<div class="col-sm-7">\n<!-- Featured Video Plus v2.2.2 -->\n<div class="featured-video-plus post-thumbnail fvp-responsive fvp-youtube fvp-center"><iframe allowfullscreen="" frameborder="0" height="506" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/1gqiSLMFOVY?autoplay&amp;list=PLIyiGQywEp67orByG2yucb66N3FtgYRnt&amp;origin=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk" width="900"></iframe></div>\n<img alt="Featured Video Play Icon" class="fvp-onload" onload="(function() {(\'initFeaturedVideoPlus\' in this) &amp;&amp; The news and editorial staff of The Times had no role in the production of this content.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<!-- pagination here -->\n<!-- the loop -->\n<div class="tracked_article bg-white" id="home-week-3">\n<div class="container">\n<div class="content-border">\n<div class="tab">\n                                WEEK 3\n                            </div>\n<div class="related-articles">\n<div class="prev-week-excerpt" style="padding-top:20px;">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow timeline week3 outer-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="scrollHorz" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-pager="&gt; .cycle-pager" data-cycle-slides="&gt; .item" data-cycle-swipe="true" data-cycle-timeout="0">\n<div class="item item-first item-bg" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div class=hidden-pager&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'88&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<h1><strong>Market-based<br>finance<br>solutions</br></br></strong></h1>\n<p>Press the arrows either side of the graphic<br>to scroll along the topic, or click a topic<br>below to jump straight to it</br></br></p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;Business&lt;br&gt;Angels&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow inner-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="scrollHorz" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-pager="&gt; .cycle-pager" data-cycle-slides="&gt; .item2" data-cycle-swipe="true" data-cycle-timeout="0">\n<div class="item2">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Business<br>Angels</br></strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-4">\n<p>Business angels refer to individual investors, usually with business experience, who provide capital for start-up firms. They are an important source of equity for small firms with growth potential in their early stages of development, long before they become attractive for venture capital funds. They provide not only financing but business experience that is vital for start-ups. Angel investment in Europe increased to \xe2\x82\xac5.5 billion in 2013, a growth of 8.7 per cent from 2012, remaining the main financier of European start-ups.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>Main areas of European early stage Investment, \xe2\x82\xacm</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/week3/xbusiness-angels.png.pagespeed.ic._M_ZKpVbTq.png">\n</img></div>\n</div>\n<p style="text-align:right; font-size:12px;padding-top:10px;">Source: EBAN (European Trade Association for Business Angels, Deed Funds and Other Early Stage Market Players)</p>\n</div>\n<div class="item2">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Business<br>Angels</br></strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>Number of active business angel networks in Europe</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/week3/xbusiness-angels-2.png.pagespeed.ic.gzk_W5ALl1.png">\n</img></div>\n</div>\n<p style="text-align:right; font-size:12px; padding-top:10px;">Source: EBAN (European Trade Association for Business Angels, Deed Funds and Other Early Stage Market Players)</p>\n</div>\n<div class="cycle-pager"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;Crowdfunding&lt;br&gt;(equity)&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow inner-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="scrollHorz" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-pager="&gt; .cycle-pager" data-cycle-slides="&gt; .item3" data-cycle-swipe="true" data-cycle-timeout="0">\n<div class="item3">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Crowdfunding<br>(equity)</br></strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>The essence of this form of funding is in the name: accumulating small investments from a large number of individuals. It usually takes the form of equity funding where each shareholder has a small stake or more often a guaranteed benefit in kind. It is generally seen as being a useful way for start-ups to obtain first stage finance and is different from peer-to-peer funding which tends to be for established business looking for loans rather than equity investment.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item3">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Crowdfunding<br>(equity)</br></strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>Estimated equity raised by crowdfunding in Europe by end 2013, \xe2\x82\xacm</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/week3/xcrowdfunding-1a2.png.pagespeed.ic.lA7HT-QjYN.png">\n<p style="text-align:right; font-size:12px;padding-top:10px;">Source: Crowdfunding Innovative Ventures in Europe, European Commission, 2013</p>\n</img></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item3">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Crowdfunding<br>(equity)</br></strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>Estimated equity raised by crowdfunding in Europe by end 2013, \xe2\x82\xacm</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/week3/xcrowdfunding-1b4.png.pagespeed.ic.vRyLGKXDsN.png">\n<p style="text-align:right; font-size:12px;padding-top:10px;">Source: Crowdfunding Innovative Ventures in Europe, European Commission, 2013</p>\n</img></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item3">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1 class="long"><strong>Crowdfunding<br>(reward/donation)</br></strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>People who choose to put money in these schemes invest simply because they believe in the cause or the wider value of the product. Investors who donate are giving money in the same way they would to a charity. Donors have a social or personal motivation for putting their money in and expect nothing back, except perhaps to feel good about helping the project. Reward crowdfunding gives investors specific benefits such as a number of free meals at a new restaurant, a number of cases of beer produced by a brewery, as acknowledgements on an album cover, tickets to an event, regular news updates, and free gifts. Returns are considered intangible.</p>\n<p>Estimated equity raised by crowdfunding in Europe by end 2013, \xe2\x82\xacm</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/week3/xcrowdfunding2.png.pagespeed.ic.5XMRh7-ZHV.png">\n<p style="text-align:right; font-size:12px;padding-top:10px;">Source: Moving Mainstream; University of Cambridge &amp; EY</p>\n</img></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="cycle-pager"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;Corperate&lt;br&gt;bonds&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow inner-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="scrollHorz" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-pager="&gt; .cycle-pager" data-cycle-slides="&gt; .item4" data-cycle-swipe="true" data-cycle-timeout="0">\n<div class="item4">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Corperate<br>bonds</br></strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>A corporate bond is an IOU issued by a company to lenders. Lenders buy a bond with a promise to be repaid a certain number of years later. They receive a fixed interest payment known as a coupon. Because corporate bonds are seen as more risky than government bonds such as so-called risk-free US Treasurys, they tend to pay a higher coupon. The bonds can be traded between investors and receive a grade from rating agencies. Bonds issued by start-ups tended to be rated as \xe2\x80\x9cjunk\xe2\x80\x9d. The backing for the bond usually comes from the company\xe2\x80\x99s ability to repay the bond, which is typically money to be earned from future operations. In some cases, the company\xe2\x80\x99s physical assets may be used as collateral for bonds. Most institutional bonds issued by companies have larger minimum investments, effectively ruling them out as individual holdings for household investors. In London retail bonds emerged to fill this gap as they usually require a minimum investment of between \xc2\xa3100 and \xc2\xa31,000.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item4">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Corperate<br>bonds</br></strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>International European Debt Capital Markets Volume</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/week3/xcorporate-bonds.png.pagespeed.ic.ZbMXqXKKRn.png">\n<p style="text-align:right; font-size:12px;padding-top:10px;">Source: Dealogic</p>\n</img></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="cycle-pager"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;Equity&lt;br&gt;finance&lt;br&gt;(shares)&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow inner-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="scrollHorz" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-pager="&gt; .cycle-pager" data-cycle-slides="&gt; .item5" data-cycle-swipe="true" data-cycle-timeout="0">\n<div class="item5">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Equity finance</strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>Companies can raise money by selling shares in the business. This can range from a start-up selling stakes in the business to friends and family through to multi-billion pound initial public offerings (IPOs or flotations) on major stock exchanges such as the London and Paris stock exchanges. In the middle are stock markets such as London\xe2\x80\x99s Alternative Investment Market that cater for smaller companies looking to raise equity finance. Investors can lose all of their investment if the company fails.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item5">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Equity finance</strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>European IPO Activity</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/week3/xequityfinance1.png.pagespeed.ic.2gCSFZ0j0x.png">\n<p style="text-align:right; font-size:12px;padding-top:10px;">Source: PwC</p>\n</img></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item5">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Equity finance</strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>AIM UK listed companies</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/week3/xequityfinance2.png.pagespeed.ic.xRKpX19YVg.png">\n<p style="text-align:right; font-size:12px;padding-top:10px;">Source: London Stock Exchange, AIM UK Market Statistics, December 2014</p>\n</img></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item5">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Equity finance</strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-4">\n<p><strong>Invoice discounting</strong></p>\n<p>Companies can raise money by passing on their unpaid invoices or trade receivables (amounts billed by a business to its customers when it delivers goods or services) to a financing company in exchange for a fee. There are two related techniques that enable businesses to raise short-term funds often for one or two months \xe2\x80\x94 factoring and invoice discounting. With factoring, the financier takes over the job of collecting the money owed which means that the debtor knows that the company has sold on the invoice. With invoice discounting, the financier lends money against your unpaid invoices, which the borrowers remain responsible for collecting.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>Total European Invoice Factor Market</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/week3/xequityfinance3.png.pagespeed.ic.jrKs0H1PWf.png">\n<p style="text-align:right; font-size:12px;padding-top:10px;">Source: A Treasurer\xe2\x80\x99s Guide to the Financial Supply Chain, Demica, 2012</p>\n</img></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="cycle-pager"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;Mini-bonds&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow inner-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="scrollHorz" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-pager="&gt; .cycle-pager" data-cycle-slides="&gt; .item6" data-cycle-swipe="true" data-cycle-timeout="0">\n<div class="item6">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Mini-bonds</strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>Mini-bonds are a category of unsecured debts that are not transferable between investors. In other words, once an investor has bought a mini bond, they cannot sell them on but have to retain them until the company buys them back after a fixed period, which is typically after three to five years. The return obtained by investors is in the form of interest, which is paid in cash and often higher than other instruments reflecting higher risk, interest paid in kind (for example, discounts on the company\xe2\x80\x99s products or services) or a mixture of the two. Italy launched a market in mini-bonds in 2012 and a number UK companies have issued mini-bonds.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item6">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Mini-bonds</strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-4">\n<p><strong>Peer-to-peer lending</strong></p>\n<p>Peer-to-peer (P2P) lending, also known as crowdlending or social lending, is a personal loan from one individual or company to another, without the involvement of a bank or other traditional financial intermediary.  P2P lending remains a tiny fraction of overall lending sources for households and SMEs, but one that has been quickly growing.  P2P lending platforms match cash flows from borrowers with those from lenders through internet platforms.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p><strong>Pensions funds</strong></p>\n<p>Pension-led funding uses directors\' personal, existing pension facilities to raise capital for their business. Funds are released from existing pensions and repaid to the scheme with interest. All funding is repaid via loan payments over a fixed term or lease payments as agreed by the trustees and at an agreed commercial rate. It provides companies an opportunity to fund growth opportunities by using assets locked up in a pension scheme.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="cycle-pager"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;Private&lt;br&gt;equity&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Private<br>equity</br></strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>Private equity firms provide medium to long-term finance in return for an equity stake in unquoted companies with high-growth potential. Because investors\xe2\x80\x99 return depends on the growth and profitability of the business, most private equity investors work with the company as a partner to grow the business. Investors look for high-growth potential businesses to invest in, and are likely to be more hands-on than banks. They can also often bring useful expertise into a business.</p>\n<p>Incremental amount of private equity funding raised</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/week3/xprivate-equity.png.pagespeed.ic.NOMdtyRbzQ.png">\n<p style="text-align:right; font-size:12px;padding-top:10px;">Source: EVCA/ PEREP Analytics</p>\n</img></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;Private&lt;br&gt;placement&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Private<br>placement</br></strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>A private placement is a long-term, typically for five to 12 years, fixed interest debt instrument, issued by a corporate directly to institutional investors, including pension funds and insurance companies. Private placements are suitable for businesses who require long-term, senior debt capital, but who are not ready for the public debt markets. The market is more developed in the United States than Europe. The volume of European private placements is growing while US cross border placements have stagnated. There is a strong market in bilateral loans in Germany privately placed, unlisted and unregistered known as Schuldschein.</p>\n<p>Private placements by European corporates</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/week3/xprivate-placement.png.pagespeed.ic.HHQkNbiHEw.png">\n<p style="text-align:right; font-size:12px;padding-top:10px;">Source: M&amp;G;, Standard &amp; Poor\xe2\x80\x99s</p>\n</img></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;Securitisation&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow inner-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="scrollHorz" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-pager="&gt; .cycle-pager" data-cycle-slides="&gt; .item7" data-cycle-swipe="true" data-cycle-timeout="0">\n<div class="item7">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1 class="long"><strong>Securitisation</strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>Also known as asset-based lending, securitisation is the idea of taking assets such as business revenue streams and using those to back the creation and issuance of tradable securities, such as bonds. A whole business securitisation involves the issue of bonds by a special purpose company, which then lends on the proceeds on a secured basis to the operating company, which holds the business assets. However SME- and asset-based securitisation make up a much smaller share of the overall market compared with mortgage-backed securities.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item7">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1 class="long"><strong>Securitisation</strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>European SME securitisation by country</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/week3/xsecuritisation.png.pagespeed.ic.OdqF3qVr_8.png">\n<p style="padding-top:10px;">Outstanding SME securitisation in selected EU countries, in \xe2\x82\xacbn and per cent of total, 2013</p>\n<p style="text-align:right; font-size:12px;padding-top:10px;">Source: AFME, SIFMA, Bloomberg, Dealogic, Thomson Reuters, prospectus filings, Fitch Ratings, Moody\xe2\x80\x99s, S&amp;P;</p>\n</img></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="cycle-pager"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;Venture&lt;br&gt;Capital&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-3">\n<div class="year">\n<h1><strong>Venture<br>Capital</br></strong></h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-9">\n<p>Venture Capital funds invest in early stage, high-risk, but high-potential businesses. It is most typically suited for early stage companies that are experiencing high-growth or have potential for high-growth. Venture capitalists tend to exit the company typically after a three- to seven-year investment by selling their shares, either back to the business or to another investor</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/week3/xventure-capital.png.pagespeed.ic.mzgK3VfCfz.png">\n</img></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<a class="cycle-prev" href="#"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xleft-arrow.png.pagespeed.ic.XCwXghIqGG.png"/></a>\n<a class="cycle-next" href="#"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xright-arrow.png.pagespeed.ic.eGABl7hkwm.png"/></a></div></div></div></div></div></div></section></body></html>\n<div class="cycle-pager"></div>\n\n\n<div class="prev-week-content">\n<h3 style="padding-top:20px;">To grow quickly, Europe\xe2\x80\x99s SMEs must seek alternative funding</h3>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-sm-8">\n<div class="main-content">\n<div class="content-pic">\n<img alt="euromarkets-wk3-1" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" data-lazy-src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/euromarkets-wk3-1.jpg" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/lazy-load/images/1x1.trans.gif"/><noscript><img alt="euromarkets-wk3-1" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/euromarkets-wk3-1.jpg"/></noscript> <p></p>\n<p></p>\n</div>\n<div class="content" id="main-content">\n<p><strong>The EU should take its lead from the US, says Phil Thornton in the third feature of a weekly four-part series.</strong></p>\n<p>Europe\xe2\x80\x99s economy is too dependent on bank finance. More than six out of ten small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) across the European Union look to their bank to provide growth capital.</p>\n<p>Bank loans and securitised assets  together make up less than 40 per cent of the \xe2\x82\xac1.15\xe2\x80\x8atrillion of outstanding funding for SMEs in the US: the equivalent share in the EU is more than 75 per cent.</p>\n<p>In the wake of the financial crisis banks have cut lending to restore their capital buffers, so small firms have not been able to find capital markets-based finance in the way that their American cousins can.</p>\n<p>In 2013 banks cut the amount they lent to SMEs, which are seen as riskier bets, by \xe2\x82\xac232\xe2\x80\x8amillion. And according to   a recent survey by the European Commission, more than a third of SMEs  failed to get the full bank financing  they wanted in the six months to  September 2014.</p>\n<p>Non-banking finance is on the rise. Finance from hedge funds, private equity, pension funds and shadow banks is growing but that is often not suited to SMEs.</p>\n<p>They are increasingly looking to what is known as alternative finance (alt-fi) and in particular online platform-based funding. Between 2012 and 2014, total alt-fi funding in Europe surged 146 per cent each year, according to a report by the Cambridge Centre   for Alternative Finance (CCAF) at the Judge Business School.</p>\n<p>Funding to consumers, entrepreneurs, creative artists, SMEs, social enterprises, renewable energy projects, community organisations and good causes across the EU rose from \xe2\x82\xac487\xe2\x80\x8amillion in 2012 to \xe2\x82\xac2.96\xe2\x80\x8abillion in 2014 and may have surpassed \xe2\x82\xac7\xe2\x80\x8abillion this year.</p>\n<p>Robert Wardrop, executive director of CCAF, said banks might be under- lending to good borrowers because their credit analytics classified fast growing companies with volatile income streams as too risky. \xe2\x80\x9cI believe these alternative channels are viable and legitimate sources of financing,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAs the market matures, companies that can borrow through bank channels may start to elect to use more alternative channels.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p>He said the real motive was the speed at which alternative lenders could respond to funding needs, rather than the price. \xe2\x80\x9cSmaller companies don\xe2\x80\x99t work on the basis of what capital they will need in 18 months\xe2\x80\x99 time. When they get an order they need the money now to produce it.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p>Much of the success of alt-fi is centred on the UK, which accounted for three quarters of the alternative  finance market during 2012-14, while the 27 remaining  members of the EU contributed 25.7 per cent.</p>\n<p>Last year, UK alt-fi processed \xe2\x82\xac2.33\xe2\x80\x8abillion of deals compared with \xe2\x82\xac154\xe2\x80\x8amillion in Germany, the second placed country. On a per capita basis, that is \xe2\x82\xac36 in the UK and \xe2\x82\xac1.70 in Germany.</p>\n<p>Mr Wardrop said a lot came down to the conservative attitudes of   German SMEs, or mittelstand. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are not sure how much they can trust these alternative channels although I think that will change over time.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p>He pointed to Estonia, the second biggest market on a per capita basis financing \xe2\x82\xac16.70 per person, which was the result of having more \xe2\x80\x9cbalanced\xe2\x80\x9d regulation between the interests of  innovation and investor protection.</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\x9cI think the UK and Estonia have done that well and Germany has not done that well. But Estonia has also high internet usage and digital ID cards, so that attitudinal shift is easier.\xe2\x80\x9d </p>\n<p>The European Commission now plans to break down barriers blocking cross-border investments to make it easier for companies to get the finance they need, regardless of where they  are located.</p>\n<p>Christian Odendahl, chief economist of the Centre for European Reform, said the goal was to diversify the funding for companies, which was  important for the sustainability of their funding base in times of crises.</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\x9cWith access to both capital market and bank funding, firms can adjust funding depending on their needs. The deeper the markets are and the more investors there are to assess the risk, the less costly it will be for borrowers.\xe2\x80\x9d </p>\n</div>\n<div class="quote hidden-xs">\n<img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xquotes.jpg.pagespeed.ic.0Ijl46bHM-.jpg"/>\n<p>With access to both capital market and bank funding, firms can adjust funding depending on their needs</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-4">\n<div class="bg-blue gt-article">\n<h5 style="margin:0;">Advertisement Feature</h5>\n<div class="gt-title">\n<h3>Comment &amp; Analysis</h3>\n<p>Jernej Omahen</p>\n<p>Head of European financial institutions research, Goldman Sachs</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/xJernej-Omahen.jpg.pagespeed.ic.mgbJ-DRyWW.jpg"/>\n<p><strong>Goldman Sachs</strong></p>\n</div>\n<div class="gt-linebreak"></div>\n<p>Should Europe\'s bolstered banks look closer to home?<br/>\n<br/>\r\nThe silver lining of the European financial crisis is that it has led to stronger, better regulated banks, whose capital positions are also much improved. On a broader level, financial stability is now guarded by newly established institutions \xe2\x80\x94 notably, a single bank supervisor (the European Central Bank). Problems remain but they are contained, and relate to a smaller number of banks.<br/>\n<br/>\r\nCrisis lessons abound. At their centre is the recognition that European banks could often do better with more focused balance sheets and reduced complexity. The new regulatory framework \xe2\x80\x94 which progressively penalises bank size \xe2\x80\x94 underlines this. <br/>\n<br/>\r\nEuropean banks have taken note, and many of the largest ones have made more effort to reduce their balance sheets. This has three main consequences. <br/>\n<br/>\r\nFirstly, because Europe\xe2\x80\x99s banks are smaller, with increasingly robust balance sheets, the financial sector as a whole is stronger.<br/>\n<br/>\r\nSecondly, having smaller banks is likely to result in less freely available bank credit. Currently, this effect is papered over by accommodative monetary policy. But in Europe, where bank financing is essentially the sole funding avenue for most corporates, this is of particular importance. Diversification in financing sources \xe2\x80\x94 away from banks and towards markets \xe2\x80\x94 would therefore benefit Europe\xe2\x80\x99s corporates, and ultimately consumers. <br/>\n<br/>\r\nThirdly, European banks\xe2\x80\x99 global importance is diminishing. As they retreat, banks struggle to identify long-term strategic alternatives. Indeed, are there any? The eurozone has created a bank union with a single supervisor at its centre. The playing field is levelling out, yet no large bank aims for a pan-eurozone presence. Perhaps, as the bank union matures, the prospect of a European retail and commercial bank at its centre will become a viable, strategic alternative.</p>\n<p class="gt-terms">This content was produced by the Advertising Department of The Times in collaboration with Goldman Sachs. The news and editorial staffs of The Times had no role in its preparation.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="share">\n<div class="a2a_kit a2a_kit_size_32 a2a_default_style" data-a2a-title="To grow quickly, Europe\xe2\x80\x99s SMEs must seek alternative funding" data-a2a-url="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/home-week-3/">\n<a
