src="//static.addtoany.com/menu/page.js"></script>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<button class="btn readmore">Read More</button>\n\n\n\n\n<!-- end of the loop -->\n<div class="tracked_article bg-white" id="the-growth-of-crowd-funding-across-the-eu">\n<div class="container">\n<div class="content-border">\n<div class="tab">\n                                        MORE FROM THE TIMES\n                                    </div>\n<h3>Why SMEs should learn to crowd surf</h3>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="related-articles">\n<div class="col-sm-10 col-sm-offset-1">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="sub-article-excerpt">\n<div class="col-sm-7">\n<img alt="City skyline made from coins, blue sky background" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" data-lazy-src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/121710105.jpg" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/lazy-load/images/1x1.trans.gif"/><noscript><img alt="City skyline made from coins, blue sky background" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/x121710105.jpg.pagespeed.ic.YweU2fLqSS.jpg"/></noscript> </div>\n<div class="col-sm-5">\n<p>Crowdfunding \xe2\x80\x93 financing something by getting small amounts of money from many individuals, rather than a large amount from one person or institution \xe2\x80\x93 may sound like an idea that sprang out of Silicon Valley, but it has a heritage stretching back centuries. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s just pooling financial resources, which people have done for hundreds of years,\xe2\x80\x9d says Oliver Gajda, the executive director of the European\xe2\x80\xa6</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="sub-article-content">\n<div class="col-sm-12">\n<img alt="City skyline made from coins, blue sky background" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" data-lazy-src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/121710105.jpg" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/lazy-load/images/1x1.trans.gif"/><noscript><img alt="City skyline made from coins, blue sky background" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/x121710105.jpg.pagespeed.ic.YweU2fLqSS.jpg"/></noscript> <p><p>Crowdfunding \xe2\x80\x93 financing something by getting small amounts of money from many individuals, rather than a large amount from one person or institution \xe2\x80\x93 may sound like an idea that sprang out of Silicon Valley, but it has a heritage stretching back centuries. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s just pooling financial resources, which people have done for hundreds of years,\xe2\x80\x9d says Oliver Gajda, the executive director of the European Crowdfunding Network, a not-for-profit organisation that represents crowdfunding platforms. \xe2\x80\x9cThe pedestal for the Statue of Liberty was financed by tens of thousands of people, each giving a small amount of money.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nEven the modern, internet form of this financing technique began not in the Valley\xe2\x80\x99s start-up circles but with charities, artists and musicians seeking donations. It was only more recently that it became a serious source of alternative finance for businesses and profit-seeking investors.<br/>\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the financial services side, especially lending, it really started to grow with the crisis of 2008,\xe2\x80\x9d Gajda says. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have seen new interest because of the distress in the banking system and the withdrawal of banks from the SME space.\xe2\x80\x9d Entrepreneurs started turning to platforms such as Crowdcube, FundedByMe and Innovestment to secure funding for their start-up or expansion plans. Some platforms specialise in debt crowdfunding, also known as peer-to-peer lending, where investors expect their money back with interest; others concentrate on equity deals, where investors get shares or a small stake in the business in return for their money; and some do both. Others focus on \xe2\x80\x9creward crowdfunding\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x93 a donation in return for a thank you from a charity, perhaps, or a mention in a new band\xe2\x80\x99s album notes online.<br/>\nThe real growth seems to be happening in lending, particularly in the UK. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is because of the regulatory framework,\xe2\x80\x9d Gajda says. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Financial Service Authority\xe2\x80\x99s approach is very liberal. Lending platforms are not seen to be doing banking activities, so they do not need a banking licence.\xe2\x80\x9d This is not true in other European nations, such as Germany, where it is a more costly endeavour, making it less attractive to entrepreneurs interested in starting up a financial technology business.<br/>\nGovernments that support crowdfunding tend to see it as a way to help the growth of SMEs and, as a result, the economy, says Dr Sovan Mitra, a lecturer in finance at the GCU London business school. \xe2\x80\x9cA lot of governments, and people, used to underestimate its importance, but now they see that it could be a key contributor to the economy,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. \xe2\x80\x9cBusinesses can obtain funding without having to go to banks, which might turn them down or impose high costs.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nHe also points out that because crowdfunding platforms are internet-based, they have an affinity for other online businesses and those in the \xe2\x80\x98knowledge economy\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x93 such as consultancies and so on \xe2\x80\x93 which traditional lenders might not share.<br/>\nThat is not to say that traditional financial institutions are ignoring the rise of crowdfunding. An increasing number of institutional investors in Europe are getting involved, in a trend that started in the US, Gajda says. \xe2\x80\x9cThe real growth is coming not from private individuals but from investment funds, high-net-worth individuals, family offices, hedge funds and banks,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich are allocating asset tranches to peer-to-peer platforms. This has allowed the industry to issue money into the market at a much greater scale.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nMitra agrees: \xe2\x80\x9cSince the crisis, financial institutions are looking at a range of different investments \xe2\x80\x93crowdfunding for instance, but also microfinance \xe2\x80\x93 to get higher returns.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nFor institutional investors, crowdfunding offers relatively high returns, but with the risks ameliorated \xe2\x80\x93 just as they are for individuals \xe2\x80\x93 by the platforms spreading their money across a selection of loans rather than allocating it to one or two. It could mean that crowdfunding platforms, or at least some of them, may soon rely much less on the crowd.<br/>\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe platforms will become less peer-to-peer lenders and more idea generators that offer a portfolio of loans. Or they may become asset-allocation providers,\xe2\x80\x9d Gajda says. Some might choose to focus on risk management by using the data from large numbers of loans plus \xe2\x80\x9csocial forecasting\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x93 a technique that uses social media to determine someone\xe2\x80\x99s risk profile.<br/>\nGajda has some advice for potential investors. He says that to minimise risk investors need a diversified portfolio. It is critical you spread your investment across multiple projects \xe2\x80\x93 something that most platforms require, or will even do for you \xe2\x80\x93 if you are looking to lend money through debt crowdfunding, Gajda says. He also advises looking at the platform\xe2\x80\x99s terms and conditions and its track record for selecting borrowers who repay their loans on time. You can expect returns of around 8 per cent, he says.<br/>\nIf you are considering equity crowdfunding, you are taking a considerably bigger risk with your money, and you need to be prepared to put a lot more work into managing your investments. The obvious risk is that the company you invest in may go under, but there is also no (official) secondary market for shares allocated this way, so it is usually only possible to recover your initial investment and realise any profits when \xe2\x80\x93 or indeed if \xe2\x80\x93 the business goes public or is sold. On the other hand, if you have the stomach for it and you get a good portfolio with two or three strong companies, Gajda says, \xe2\x80\x9cyou could make a 500 per cent return\xe2\x80\x9d.</p>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="share">\n<div class="a2a_kit a2a_kit_size_32 a2a_default_style" data-a2a-title="Why SMEs should learn to crowd surf" data-a2a-url="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/week3/the-growth-of-crowd-funding-across-the-eu/">\n<a class="a2a_button_facebook"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_twitter"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_google_plus"></a>\n<a class="a2a_dd" href="https://www.addtoany.com/share"></a>\n</div>\n<script async="" src="//static.addtoany.com/menu/page.js"></script>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p style="color:#000;font-size:14px;margin-top:15px;"></p>\n</div>\n<button class="btn readmore">Read More</button>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="bg-blue" id="a-dialogue-on-creating-an-eu-capital-markets-union">\n<div class="container">\n<div class="content-border">\n<div class="tab">\n                                        VIDEO\n                                    </div>\n<h3 class="tracked_port">A dialogue on creating an EU capital markets union</h3>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="related-articles">\n<div class="col-sm-10 col-sm-offset-1">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="sub-article-excerpt">\n<div class="col-sm-7">\n<div class="fluid-width-video-wrapper" style="min-height:260px;">\n<!-- Featured Video Plus v2.2.2 -->\n<div class="featured-video-plus post-thumbnail fvp-responsive fvp-youtube fvp-center"><iframe allowfullscreen="" frameborder="0" height="506" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/yWQ3lZym-S8?autoplay&amp;list=PLIyiGQywEp67orByG2yucb66N3FtgYRnt&amp;origin=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk" width="900"></iframe></div>\n<img alt="Featured Video Play Icon" class="fvp-onload" onload="(function() {(\'initFeaturedVideoPlus\' in this) &amp;&amp; (\'function\' === typeof initFeaturedVideoPlus) &amp;&amp; initFeaturedVideoPlus();})();" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/featured-video-plus/img/playicon.png"/> </div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-5">\n<p>Jim Esposito, co-head of the Global Financing Group at Goldman Sachs, discusses the need for deeper and more liquid capital markets in Europe to help support European economic growth.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="sub-article-content">\n<div class="col-sm-12">\n<!-- Featured Video Plus v2.2.2 -->\n<div class="featured-video-plus post-thumbnail fvp-responsive fvp-youtube fvp-center"><iframe allowfullscreen="" frameborder="0" height="506" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/yWQ3lZym-S8?autoplay&amp;list=PLIyiGQywEp67orByG2yucb66N3FtgYRnt&amp;origin=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk" width="900"></iframe></div>\n<img alt="Featured Video Play Icon" class="fvp-onload" onload="(function() {(\'initFeaturedVideoPlus\' in this) &amp;&amp; (\'function\' === typeof initFeaturedVideoPlus) &amp;&amp; initFeaturedVideoPlus();})();" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/featured-video-plus/img/playicon.png"/> <p><p>Jim Esposito, co-head of the Global Financing Group at Goldman Sachs, discusses the need for deeper and more liquid capital markets in Europe to help support European economic growth.</p>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="share">\n<div class="a2a_kit a2a_kit_size_32 a2a_default_style" data-a2a-title="A dialogue on creating an EU capital markets union" data-a2a-url="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/week3/a-dialogue-on-creating-an-eu-capital-markets-union/">\n<a class="a2a_button_facebook"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_twitter"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_google_plus"></a>\n<a class="a2a_dd" href="https://www.addtoany.com/share"></a>\n</div>\n<script async="" src="//static.addtoany.com/menu/page.js"></script>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p style="color:#000;font-size:14px;margin-top:15px;">Content provided by Goldman Sachs. The news and editorial staff of The Times had no role in the production of this content.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<!-- pagination here -->\n<div class="bg-blue">\n<div class="container">\n<div class="content-border">\n<div class="tab">\n                                    DATA\n                                </div>\n<a href="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/12/EU-Growth_R12-1-2.pdf" target="_blank"><h3 class="tracked_port">Unlocking Europe\'s economic potential</h3></a>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-sm-10 col-sm-offset-1">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-sm-7">\n<a href="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/12/EU-Growth_R12-1-2.pdf" target="_blank"><img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xadditional-img.jpg.pagespeed.ic.Uh1QyzpHUy.jpg" style="margin-bottom:15px;"/></a>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-5">\n<p style="padding-top:0;"><a href="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/12/EU-Growth_R12-1-2.pdf" style="color:#000" target="_blank">Europe\'s recovery from the financial crisis has been relatively slow. The European economy is now growing again, but at a still modest pace, and therefore further efforts are required to strengthen the region\'s economy.</a></p>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p style="color:#000;font-size:14px;margin-top:15px;"></p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<!-- pagination here -->\n<!-- the loop -->\n<div class="tracked_article bg-white" id="home-week-2">\n<div class="container">\n<div class="content-border">\n<div class="tab">\n                                WEEK 2\n                            </div>\n<div class="related-articles">\n<div class="prev-week-excerpt" style="padding-top:20px;">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow timeline" data-cycle-fx="scrollHorz" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-pager="&gt; .cycle-pager" data-cycle-pause-on-hover="true" data-cycle-slides="&gt; .item" data-cycle-swipe="true" data-cycle-timeout="5000">\n<div class="item item-first item-bg" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div class=hidden-pager&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'88&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'89</h1></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div>-\'15\n\n\n<div class="col-md-10">\n<h1><strong>Timeline:</strong></h1></div>The Europeanmonetary union\n<p>1989 - 2015</p>\n<p>Press the arrows to scroll along the timeline,</p>or click a year below to jump to it.\n                                \n\n\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'89&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'89</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>A committee established by the then European Commission president Jacques Delors to look at the final stage of economic and monetary union recommends a three-stage process:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Complete the internal market and remove restrictions on further financial integration;</li>\n<li>Establish a European Monetary Institute to strengthen central bank co-operation and plan the transition to the euro;\n                                        </li>\n<li>Fix final exchange rates and transition to the euro, establish the European Central bank and implement binding rules to govern the size and financing of national budget deficits.</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>Their unanimous report defines the objective of monetary union as: a complete liberalisation of capital movements; full integration of financial markets; irreversible convertibility of currencies; irrevocable fixing of exchange rates; and the possible replacement of national currencies with a single currency.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'90&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'90</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-10">\n<p>The UK joins the exchange rate mechanism (ERM), a semi-pegged exchange rate system that EU members needed to stay within ahead of joining the euro.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'91&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'91</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-10">\n<p>European leaders sign the Maastricht Treaty that creates the European Union and sets out a timetable for economic and monetary union. Governments must meet convergence criteria on budget deficits, public debts, inflation, exchange rate stability and long-term interest rates. The UK secures an opt out from the euro while Denmark was given the right to decide if and when it would join the euro.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'92&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'92</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-10">\n<p>Danish voters reject the Maastricht Treaty, it passes in all other countries. Black Wednesday (16 September) sees Britain forced to withdraw sterling from the ERM after it is unable to keep sterling above its agreed lower limit.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'93&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'93</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-10">\n<p>The European Single Market establishes the free movement of goods, services, people and capital. Spain, Portugal and Ireland devalue their currencies while speculators attack the French franc. In response EU leaders agree to widen the range within which ERM currencies are allowed to fluctuate to 15 per cent either side of central rates.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'94&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'94</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-10">\n<p>Stage 2 of EMU establishes the European Monetary Institute, an embryonic version of a central bank. Member states agree to introduce the euro as a currency in January 1999 at the latest, but with a transition period before national bank notes and coins are replaced.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'95&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'95</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-10">\n<p>Austria, Finland and Sweden join the EU. European leaders agree to call the new single currency the euro.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'96&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'96</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-10">\n<p>The Finnish Mark joins \xe2\x80\x94 and the Italian lira re-enters \xe2\x80\x94 the exchange rate mechanism.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'97&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'97</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>EU governments set up a stability and growth pact to enforce budgetary discipline among euro area member states. It was proposed by Germany which wanted to legislate for fiscal responsibility, and limit the ability of governments to exert inflationary pressures on the wider European economy.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>In the UK the newly-elected Labour government set five economic tests that must be met for the country to adopt the euro.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'98&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'98</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>European Commission recommends the 11 countries to participate in the first wave of monetary union: Austria, Belgium, France, Finland, Germany, Luxembourg, Ireland, Netherlands, Spain, Italy and Portugal.</p>\n<p>The exchange rates of national currencies of the countries converting to the euro are permanently fixed on 31 December.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>The European Central Bank is established in Frankfurt under Wim Duisenberg of the Netherlands as its first president.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'99&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'99</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-10">\n<p>The euro becomes a real currency with its launch in electronic form on 1 January for the 11 countries. Greece is deemed too weak to join while Denmark, Sweden and the UK choose to stay out. The ECB assumes responsibility for monetary policy from individual central banks.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'00&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'00</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>The European Central Bank, supported by the central banks of the United States and Japan, intervenes in the currency markets to prop up the value of the euro, which at one point falls to a low of $0.8875.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>The Danes vote no to adopting the single currency by 53% to 47% despite the overwhelming support for the yes campaign by most of the political parties, media and industry.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'01&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'01</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-10">\n<p>Greece joins the eurozone as its 12th member. It subsequently transpires that the public finances data supplied to support entry were inaccurate.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'02&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'02</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-10">\n<p>Euro notes and coins are introduced. Initially the national currencies of the 12 members remain legal tender but on 1 July national coins and banknotes in member countries are no longer valid and euros become the sole legal currency.</p>\n<p>Sweden votes in a referendum not to adopt the euro.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'03&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'03</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>The Stability and Growth Pact, whose budget deficit limit of 3% of GDP had been repeatedly breached by France and Germany, is modified to allow for excessive deficits to be excused for \xe2\x80\x9crelevant factors\xe2\x80\x9d. These included increased aid spending in the third world and the costs of German reunification. There are no sanctions for \xe2\x80\x9csmall and temporary\xe2\x80\x9d breaches.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>The UK says that its five economic tests for joining the euro have not been met.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'04&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'04</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-10">\n<p>Eight central and eastern European countries - the Czech Republic, Estonia, Hungary, Latvia, Lithuania, Poland, Slovakia and Slovenia - known as the A8 (Accession 8) join the EU along with Cyprus and Malta.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'05&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'05</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>In May French voters reject plans to ratify the proposed Constitution of Europe by a margin of 55% to 45% in a referendum.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>In June in the Netherlands 61.6% of voters rejected the Constitution in a non-binding referendum. The constitution was abandoned in favour of what became the Treaty of Lisbon.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'06&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'06</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-md-10">\n<p>The New York Stock Exchange made a $10.2bn cash and shares bid for Euronext NV, a European exchange operator, in an bid to become the world\xe2\x80\x99s first transatlantic stock trading centre. The two merged completed the merger in 2007.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'07&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'07</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>Bulgaria and Romania join the EU. Slovenia adopts the euro.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>The Markets in Financial Instruments Directive takes effect on 1 November after seven years of regulatory development and consultation.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'08&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'08</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>Malta and Cyprus join the euro.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>EU leaders agree on a \xe2\x82\xac200 billion stimulus plan to help boost European growth following the global financial crisis.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'09&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'09</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>Slovakia joins the euro while Estonia, Denmark, Latvia and Lithuania join the ERM to bring their currencies and monetary policy into line with the euro in preparation for joining.</p>\n<p>The Treaty of Lisbon entered into force on 1 December 2009.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>In December Greece admits that its debts have reached \xe2\x82\xac300 billion pushing its debt/GDP ratio to 113%, almost twice the Maastricht limit of 60%. Ratings agencies start to downgrade Greek debt and the risk of a eurozone government default surfaces for the first time, forcing the prime minister George Papandreou to insist that Greece is not about to default on its debts.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'10&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'10</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>The EU announces that Greece\xe2\x80\x99s deficit in 2009 was 13.6% of GDP rather than 3.7% as first reported. It tells Greece to make further spending cuts. The EU and the International Monetary Fund set up a \xe2\x82\xac110 billion rescue package for Greece in return for strict austerity measures and structural economic reforms. The subsequent austerity plans spark strikes and riots in the streets.</p>\n<p>Speculation of potential eurozone default spreads to Portugal, Ireland and Spain as well as Greece promoting analysts to use the word PIGS to refer to the weak members.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>EU member states agree to establish the European Financial Stabilisation Mechanism (EFSM) a facility to provide loans to EU Member States in financial difficulty. It is financed by borrowing against the EU Budget up to a total of \xe2\x82\xac60bn. Meanwhile eurozone governments create a temporary \xe2\x82\xac440bn European Financial Stability Facility (EFSF) for member states in difficulty.</p>\n<p>The ECB announces it will buy eurozone government bonds in order to stabilise the region\xe2\x80\x99s bond markets and protect its unity.</p>\n<p>EU approved \xe2\x82\xac85 billion rescue plan for Ireland, which passes a tough austerity budget.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'11&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'11</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>Amid the turmoil, Estonia joins the euro, taking the number of countries with the single currency to 17.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>The eurozone agree a \xe2\x82\xac78 billion bailout of Portugal. The EU, IMF and ECB agree a second bailout of \xe2\x82\xac109 billion for Greece aimed at resolving that country\xe2\x80\x99s crisis and preventing contagion to other eurozone economies.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'12&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'12</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>Spain makes a formal request for up to \xe2\x82\xac100 billion in loans from eurozone funds to shore up its financial sector. As the funds would only go to banks, there are no austerity requirements attached.</p>\n<p>Cyprus becomes the fifth eurozone country to seek a bailout after the Greek crisis exposes the fragility of its banking sector. Banks are shut for two weeks and capital controls enforced while the \xe2\x82\xac10 billion rescue package is negotiated.</p>\n<p>ECB President Mario Draghi, pledges to do \xe2\x80\x9cwhatever it takes\xe2\x80\x9d to protect the eurozone from collapse - including fighting unreasonably high government borrowing costs. He later unveils the Outright Monetary Transactions programme, an unlimited but sterilised \xe2\x80\x94 meaning it does not add to the money supply \xe2\x80\x94 bond-buying programme.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>A new permanent crisis mechanism of around \xe2\x82\xac500bn, the European Stability Mechanism (ESM), is inaugurated to build on the EFSF and focuses on prevention and will substantially reduce the probability of a crisis emerging in the future. It replaces the EFSF.</p>\n<p>EU leaders announce that the ECB will be given a supervisory role over the eurozone\xe2\x80\x99s 6,000 banks. This will enable the ESM to recapitalise banks directly, rather than lending money to sovereigns to lend on to financial institutions.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'13&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'13</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>The European Parliament approves the EU bank supervision system, which will bring some 150 of the EU\xe2\x80\x99s largest banks under the European Central Bank\'s direct oversight from September 2014.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>The ECB cuts its main refinancing interest rate by 25 basis points to 0.25%.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'14&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'14</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>Greek unemployment reaches a record high of 28%. Eurozone finance ministers release more than \xe2\x82\xac8 billion of bailout funds to Greece.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>Eurozone ministers approve a two-month extension to Greece\'s bailout, which was set to end 31 December, amid an ongoing dispute over budget provisions between Athens and its EU-IMF creditors.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="item" data-cycle-pager-template="&lt;div&gt;&lt;a href=#&gt;&lt;span&gt;\xe2\x80\xa2&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;\'15&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-md-2">\n<div class="year">\n<h1>\'15</h1>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>In Greece Syriza wins a snap general election on a mandate of resisting austerity and negotiating debt relief by the EU, IMF and the ECB.</p>\n<p>ECB announces a quantitative easing programme under which it will buy \xe2\x82\xac60 billion of sovereign debt every months from this March until the end of September 2016 creating around \xe2\x82\xac1 trillion of new money.</p>\n<p>Greek authorities send a request for a six-month extension to its EU loan programme, but leaders reject the plan. Greece fails to meet the deadline to repay \xe2\x82\xac1.54 billion to the IMF.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-6 col-md-5">\n<p>Prime minister Alexis Tsipras walks out of negotiations with the creditors and calls a referendum among Green voters of whether to accept or reject the creditors\xe2\x80\x99 latest offer that had in any event expired. Greeks vote by 63% to 37% to reject the creditors\xe2\x80\x99 offer.</p>\n<p>Tsipras makes a formal bid for new three-year ESM loan that is the centre of a series of weekend meetings in July between the leaders and finance ministers of the eurozone countries. It culminates in the offer of a third \xe2\x82\xac89 billion bailout for Greece in exchange for a tough programme of economic reforms including hikes in VAT, cuts to pension entitlements and privatisation of \xe2\x82\xac50 billion of state-owned assets.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<a class="cycle-prev" href="#"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xleft-arrow.png.pagespeed.ic.XCwXghIqGG.png"/></a>\n<a class="cycle-next" href="#"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xright-arrow.png.pagespeed.ic.eGABl7hkwm.png"/></a>\n<div class="cycle-pager" style="display:none;"></div>\n\n\n<div class="prev-week-content">\n<h3 style="padding-top:20px;">Regulation of capital market union was put on back burner</h3>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-sm-8">\n<div class="main-content">\n<div class="content-pic">\n<img alt="Euro sign on the world map background with DOF effect." class="img-responsive wp-post-image" data-lazy-src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/eurozone-wk2-1.jpg" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/lazy-load/images/1x1.trans.gif"/><noscript><img alt="Euro sign on the world map background with DOF effect." class="img-responsive wp-post-image" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/xeurozone-wk2-1.jpg.pagespeed.ic.P56l5-NcOD.jpg"/></noscript> <p></p>\n<p></p>\n</div>\n<div class="content" id="main-content">\n<p><strong>Europe has learnt from the financial crisis, says Phil Thornton in the second of a weekly four-part series.</strong></p>\n<p>The modern European Union may have been forged 65 years ago by the establishment of the European Coal and Steel Community, designed to prevent a country mobilising its armed forces without others knowing, but political leaders have always aimed at creating a seamless capital market.</p>\n<p>The opening declaration of the 1957 Treaty of Rome includes a pledge to abolish all obstacles to the free movement of goods, people, services and capital.</p>\n<p>One article specifically called on member states to \xe2\x80\x9cprogressively abolish all restrictions on the movement of capital belonging to persons resident in member states and any discrimination based on nationality or on the place where such capital is invested\xe2\x80\x9d.</p>\n<p>But while the legislation set out the ambition, it took time for policymakers to pass specific laws to make it easier for Europeans to move capital across borders, whether as a bank or as an individual saver or borrower.</p>\n<p>The creation of the single banking licence in 1989 started the process towards convergence in financial legislation and regulation across member countries, while the launch of the euro in 1999 eliminated currency risk.</p>\n<p>In the same year, 1999, Brussels launched the Financial Services Action Plan to create a single market for financial services. This culminated in the Markets in Financial Instruments Directive (MiFID) that took effect in November 2007 \xe2\x80\x94 50 years after the Treaty of Rome. It aimed to increase investors\xe2\x80\x99 choice by fostering competition, while at the same time increasing the levels of protection afforded to them.</p>\n<p>Yet even by 2004, cross-border deals accounted for only about 20 per cent of the total value of deals within the financial sector \xe2\x80\x94 less than half the average of other sectors where the proportion equalled 45 per cent.</p>\n<p>More importantly, the system of cross-border regulation failed to keep pace with these dynamic developments. While leaders realised that there would probably have to be some joint regulation, the process remained on the back burner until the global financial crisis struck.</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\x9cIn time-honoured European fashion, the idea was that you would find out over time where you needed the joint regulation and where you could have competing national regulations,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holger Schmieding, chief economist at Berenberg Bank.</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\x9cUntil the start of the systemic crisis in 2011 there was not much of a reason to do anything, as things seemed to be working pretty well.<br/>\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was only when bond yield spreads soared again after the financial crisis that the question arose: \xe2\x80\x98Is there any pieces of the architecture missing?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p>There were. The missing elements included the lack of a lender of last resort for a failing bank and a system that resulted in national bank regulators being responsible for supervising large banking groups whose main business was cross-border.</p>\n<p>When the crash came, the system was found wanting. The rescue of multinational banks, such as Dexia and Fortis of Belgium, required the governments involved to devise ad hoc burden-sharing agreements after the event.</p>\n<p>As a major study by leading academics at the Centre for Economic Policy Research concluded in 2011: \xe2\x80\x9cThe national nature of bank rescue operations has meant that there has been a reconsideration of the benefits and costs of cross-border banking.</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\x9cAn important policy lesson to draw from the crisis is to build a European-level regulatory system that will better support the emergence of a stable European banking system that includes a major role for European-wide major banking groups.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p>Since the financial crisis struck in 2008, the European Commission has embarked on about 40 new regulations to build what it calls a \xe2\x80\x9csingle rulebook for a single market\xe2\x80\x9d, while MiFID has gone through a comprehensive review. MiFID II should become law on January 3, 2017, although there are rumours it may be delayed by a year.</p>\n<p>Schmieding said that European politicians had not seen these rules as a priority, since Europe had largely been free of financial crises.</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\x9cIn a perfect world they should have done but, having said that, you learn by crisis. Even a perfectly built house can encounter a storm and once in a while the roof may get leaky if the storm is very bad. But the eurozone will be much less at risk of falling prey to a storm than it was before.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n</div>\n<div class="quote hidden-xs">\n<img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xquotes.jpg.pagespeed.ic.0Ijl46bHM-.jpg"/>\n<p>Since the financial crisis struck in 2008, the European Commission has embarked on about  40 new regulations to build what it calls a \xe2\x80\x9csingle rulebook for a single market\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-4">\n<div class="bg-blue gt-article">\n<h5 style="margin:0;">Advertisement Feature</h5>\n<div class="gt-title">\n<h3>Comment &amp; Analysis</h3>\n<p>Francesco Garzarelli</p>\n<p>Co-head of global macro market research</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/xFrancesco-Garzarelli.jpg.pagespeed.ic.CeuZmkUo5H.jpg"/>\n<p><strong>Goldman Sachs</strong></p>\n</div>\n<div class="gt-linebreak"></div>\n<p>The European banking and sovereign crisis of 2010-12 exposed the underlying fragilities of the eurozone\xe2\x80\x99s financial system. Although we have achieved a degree of stability since then, more work needs to be done before we can reach deep and integrated credit markets across the currency area.<br/>\n<br/>\r\nWhen significant economic and financial shocks hit home (such as the Great Recession in America and the Greek default), the legal and technical infrastructure of the eurozone proved inadequate to promote an orderly reduction and restructuring of the financial claims agreed during the boom. This led to even more disruption in the credit market, which further worsened the economic downturn. <br/>\n<br/>\r\nThe European Central Bank (ECB) stepped in as the lender of last resort and offered credit to the banks on very favourable terms. This allowed debtors to reduce their obligations without tipping the economy into depression. The ECB\xe2\x80\x99s support is still in place \xe2\x80\x93 now in the form of quantitative easing \xe2\x80\x93 and expected to continue well into 2017. <br/>\n<br/>\r\nSome progress has undoubtedly been made since then in addressing some of the eurozone\xe2\x80\x99s structural deficiencies. Banks are now better capitalised, and they undergo regular stress-testing to make sure they have an adequate ratio of capital to lending. If there is the risk of a bank collapse in the future, there are clear procedures to manage its orderly resolution without tapping into public finances. At the sovereign level, facilities like the European Stability Mechanism have been developed, allowing states to borrow under joint names and lend to each other on conditional terms. <br/>\n<br/>\r\nBut more work needs to be done to reduce the segmentation of the credit markets in the euro area. As part of the banking union \xe2\x80\x93 in which responsibility for banking policy was transferred from national governments to the EU \xe2\x80\x93 one of the key features agreed was a common minimum guarantee for bank deposits, but this is still not in place. And there is still a tie between banks and the nation where they are based because last-resort capital injections come from national resources, rather than supranational ones. <br/>\n<br/>\r\nThere are also many systems that vary across countries \xe2\x80\x93 for example, some financial services are subject to national barriers to entry, and bankruptcy codes and foreclosure procedures still differ across jurisdictions.<br/>\n</p>\n<p class="gt-terms">This content was produced by the Advertising Department of The Times in collaboration with Goldman Sachs. The news and editorial staffs of The Times had no role in its preparation.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="share">\n<div class="a2a_kit a2a_kit_size_32 a2a_default_style" data-a2a-title="Regulation of capital market union was put on back burner" data-a2a-url="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/home-week-2/">\n<a class="a2a_button_facebook"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_twitter"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_google_plus"></a>\n<a class="a2a_dd" href="https://www.addtoany.com/share"></a>\n</div>\n<script async="" src="//static.addtoany.com/menu/page.js"></script>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<button class="btn readmore">Read More</button>\n\n\n\n\n<!-- end of the loop -->\n<div class="tracked_article bg-white" id="what-is-the-european-commission-doing-to-improve-the-access-to-financing-for-start-ups-and-smes">\n<div class="container">\n<div class="content-border">\n<div class="tab">\n                                        MORE FROM THE TIMES\n                                    </div>\n<h3>What is the European Commission doing to improve the access to financing for start-ups and SMEs?</h3>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="related-articles">\n<div class="col-sm-10 col-sm-offset-1">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="sub-article-excerpt">\n<div class="col-sm-7">\n<img alt="Goldman-sachs-1444px-by-962px-" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" data-lazy-src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Goldman-sachs-1444px-by-962px-.jpg" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/lazy-load/images/1x1.trans.gif"/><noscript><img alt="Goldman-sachs-1444px-by-962px-" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/xGoldman-sachs-1444px-by-962px-.jpg.pagespeed.ic.rrFKe46fVI.jpg"/></noscript> </div>\n<div class="col-sm-5">\n<p><strong>David Muxworthy is adamant that without the EU\xe2\x80\x99s financial assistance, he would have been forced to give up more of the equity in his company to private investors.</strong> He is the chief financial officer of MyPinPad, a state-of-the-art technology company that specialises in authentication solutions for devices like mobiles and tablets. According to this year\xe2\x80\x99s European Parliament annual report, there are around 22 million SMEs\xe2\x80\xa6</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="sub-article-content">\n<div class="col-sm-12">\n<img alt="Goldman-sachs-1444px-by-962px-" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" data-lazy-src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Goldman-sachs-1444px-by-962px-.jpg" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/lazy-load/images/1x1.trans.gif"/><noscript><img alt="Goldman-sachs-1444px-by-962px-" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/xGoldman-sachs-1444px-by-962px-.jpg.pagespeed.ic.rrFKe46fVI.jpg"/></noscript> <p><p><strong>David Muxworthy is adamant that without the EU\xe2\x80\x99s financial assistance, he would have been forced to give up more of the equity in his company to private investors.</strong> He is the chief financial officer of MyPinPad, a state-of-the-art technology company that specialises in authentication solutions for devices like mobiles and tablets.</p>\n<p>According to this year\xe2\x80\x99s European Parliament annual report, there are around 22 million SMEs (small and medium-sized enterprises) like Muxworthy\xe2\x80\x99s operating in the EU, providing two thirds of private sector employment \xe2\x80\x93 around 75 million jobs. The International Monetary Fund describes these sorts of businesses \xe2\x80\x93 agile, innovative, entrepreneurial, job-creating and growing \xe2\x80\x93 as the \xe2\x80\x9cbackbone\xe2\x80\x9d of the European economy. The EU is well aware of SMEs\xe2\x80\x99 importance and has set up a series of financial organisations to help them fulfil their potential. Localisation is a key consideration, and focus is often given to geographical economic \xe2\x80\x9cclusters\xe2\x80\x9d.</p>\n<p>Over the past three years, the MyPinPad team have been grateful beneficiaries of this concentration on localisation, in the form of the UK government\xe2\x80\x99s Smart Specialisation strategy. This integrates investment within a local economy, helping to build more prosperous communities by identifying SMEs that can link together in close proximity.</p>\n<p>Muxworthy says: \xe2\x80\x9cWe wanted to be true to our roots and set up an innovative global fintech (financial technology) company in South Wales. There is a huge entrepreneurial spirit in Cardiff and, although the area has traditionally been overlooked for investment, there has been a shift in terms of funding \xe2\x80\x93 EU grants are available through the Welsh Structural Funds programmes and Cardiff city council.</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\x9cThey can be used for training, support, subsidies for premises with high-speed digital facilities, logistical help or funding \xe2\x80\x93 such as the \xc2\xa350,000 we needed \xe2\x80\x93 to get a business off the ground. It\xe2\x80\x99s very lonely and precarious starting your own business, but you get an incredible boost when a government agency or financial goliath shows confidence in you.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p>This sort of government support plays a large role in Europe. The business culture is far more risk averse than in America \xe2\x80\x93 where there is a good supply of venture capital, private equity and angel investment \xe2\x80\x93 and mostly relies on bank loans. However, since the financial crisis there has been a reduced credit supply, tighter credit standards and higher bank loan costs. </p>\n<p>In that environment, the EU\xe2\x80\x99s Investment Plan is another initiative that offers a boon to SMEs. It incorporates a series of measures designed to improve access to finance and markets, as well as boost innovation and competition. </p>\n<p>European Structural and Investment Funds (ESIFs) are the first measure. They involve co-financing projects at the national and regional level in a bid to increase regional competitiveness and wealth creation. The goal is to create hubs of specialist businesses \xe2\x80\x93 such as those focusing on research and innovation or fintech \xe2\x80\x93 by allowing start-ups to apply directly to local authorities. Much of the money for this comes from the European Regional Development Fund, while the European Social Fund finances education and training for entrepreneurs and their employees. British SMEs working on rural or maritime projects can also benefit from two specialist ESIF funds.</p>\n<p>The second measure is the European Investment Bank (EIB), a non-profit organisation owned by EU member states that borrows money on capital markets to lend on projects (\xc2\xa354 billion in 2014) in areas such as infrastructure and regional development. It has an SME-focused arm, the European Investment Fund. Capital from these sources is processed by local financial institutions which then provide loans to SMEs, often on preferential terms. These are usually financial instruments that complement more traditional grants supplied by banks, such as venture capital funds, private equity and angel investors.</p>\n<p>Jonathan Taylor, a vice president at the EIB, says: \xe2\x80\x9cLast year the EIB group provided \xc2\xa315 billion for lending to SMEs, including backing for venture capital, equity, tech transfer, securitisation, lending through high-street banks and microfinance. But we need continued innovation to respond to investment needs. In that vein, we\xe2\x80\x99re preparing to lend \xc2\xa3100 million to UK small businesses through the peer-to-peer lender Funding Circle, and we hope to replicate this model in other countries.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p>Thirdly, there are schemes where businesses apply to specific EU programmes for funding managed by the European Commission. The largest two for SMEs are Horizon 2020 and Cosme (Competitiveness of Enterprises and Small and Medium-Sized Enterprises). Horizon 2020 supports projects in innovation, advanced technology and the environment, while Cosme aims to widen SME access to the financial markets by, for instance, providing guarantees or risk capital to equity funds.</p>\n<p>As well as financial assistance, the EU offers more help in the form of business-support schemes, in areas such as training, IT, cross-border regulations, administration and late payments.</p>\n<p>Despite all of the potential support on offer to SMEs in Europe, there are still problems, says David Muxworthy, mostly due to bureaucracy: \xe2\x80\x9cIt takes time to access the money, there are endless forms to fill in and due diligence can also take ages; sometimes it\xe2\x80\x99s six months before you see a penny. It\xe2\x80\x99s complicated, too \xe2\x80\x93 there are so many different avenues and no one\xe2\x80\x99s bringing it all together in a one-stop shop.</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\x9cThe rewards can be great but the system is inefficient \xe2\x80\x93 and if you don\xe2\x80\x99t know where to find the money, many are inclined to give up, or to rely on banks and private equity.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="share">\n<div class="a2a_kit a2a_kit_size_32 a2a_default_style" data-a2a-title="What is the European Commission doing to improve the access to financing for start-ups and SMEs?" data-a2a-url="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/week2/what-is-the-european-commission-doing-to-improve-the-access-to-financing-for-start-ups-and-smes/">\n<a class="a2a_button_facebook"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_twitter"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_google_plus"></a>\n<a class="a2a_dd" href="https://www.addtoany.com/share"></a>\n</div>\n<script async="" src="//static.addtoany.com/menu/page.js"></script>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p style="color:#000;font-size:14px;margin-top:15px;"></p>\n</div>\n<button class="btn readmore">Read More</button>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="bg-blue" id="european-crossroads">\n<div class="container">\n<div class="content-border">\n<div class="tab">\n                                        VIDEO\n                                    </div>\n<h3 class="tracked_port">European Crossroads</h3>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="related-articles">\n<div class="col-sm-10 col-sm-offset-1">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="sub-article-excerpt">\n<div class="col-sm-7">\n<div class="fluid-width-video-wrapper" style="min-height:260px;">\n<!-- Featured Video Plus v2.2.2 -->\n<div class="featured-video-plus post-thumbnail fvp-responsive fvp-youtube fvp-center"><iframe allowfullscreen="" frameborder="0" height="506" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/VGX7L-gV3xA?autoplay&amp;list=PLIyiGQywEp67orByG2yucb66N3FtgYRnt&amp;origin=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk" width="900"></iframe></div>\n<img alt="Featured Video Play Icon" class="fvp-onload" onload="(function() {(\'initFeaturedVideoPlus\' in this) &amp;&amp; (\'function\' === typeof initFeaturedVideoPlus) &amp;&amp; with more than 30 leading CEOs in Europe, Richard Tufft, region head of EMEA Equity Research at Goldman Sachs, outlines the challenges facing the region\xe2\x80\x99s economy and shares insights on how It has also emerged as the political centre of Europe. Industry makes up more than a quarter of the economy and it is a major exporter of machinery, vehicles, chemicals, and household equipment. It benefits from a highly skilled labour force but faces a demographic challenge from an ageing population.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" Monetary Fund; European Union. All data 2014 except contributions to EU budget which are for 2013.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="selected-info week-1-info" id="uk">\n<div class="close"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xclose.png.pagespeed.ic.jrZDHn-p-l.png"/></div>\n<div class="country"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/uk/xuk.png.pagespeed.ic.dlEhMWYiux.png"/><p><strong>United Kingdom</strong><br>Capital : <strong>London</strong><br>Joined EU: 1973</br></br></p></div>\n<div class="content">\n<p>The UK is the second largest economy in Europe after Germany and is one of the three members to have an opt-out from joining the euro alongside Denmark and Sweden. Services, especially banking, insurance, and business services, are key drivers of growth and London is seen as the financial centre of Europe.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-next="#next-uk" data-cycle-prev="#prev-uk" data-cycle-slides="&gt; .item" data-cycle-swipe="true" Monetary Fund; European Union. All data 2014 except contributions to EU budget which are for 2013.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="selected-info week-1-info" id="cyprus">\n<div class="close"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xclose.png.pagespeed.ic.jrZDHn-p-l.png"/></div>\n<div class="country"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/cyprus/xcyprus.png.pagespeed.ic._-YYumusWq.png"/><p><strong>Cyprus</strong><br>Capital : <strong>Nicosia</strong><br>Joined EU: 2004</br></br></p></div>\n<div class="content">\n<p>Cyprus joined the EU as a de facto divided island but the whole of Cyprus is EU territory. This means Turkish Cypriots are EU citizens as they are citizens of an EU country. The economy is dominated by the services sector, which accounts for some 80% of GDP with tourism, financial services, and real estate playing the most important roles. In July 2012, Cyprus became the fifth eurozone government to request an economic bailout program from the European Commission, European Central Bank and the International Monetary Fund. The bailout led to a two-week bank closure and the imposition of capital controls that were only completely withdrawn in April 2015.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" Although it is obliged to seek admission to the euro it has said that it is only a voluntary member of the exchange rate mechanism and has chosen to remain outside pending public approval by a referendum. Industry makes up a fifth of the economy and includes specialisms in iron and steel, precision equipment and autos.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" Monetary Fund; European Union. All data 2014 except contributions to EU budget which are for 2013.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="selected-info week-1-info" id="spain">\n<div class="close"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xclose.png.pagespeed.ic.jrZDHn-p-l.png"/></div>\n<div class="country"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/spain/xspain.png.pagespeed.ic.C89XTRZrtQ.png"/><p><strong>Spain</strong><br>Capital : <strong>Madrid</strong><br>Joined EU: 1986</br></br></p></div>\n<div class="content">\n<p>Since joining the EU in 1986, the Spanish economy had been one of the most dynamic in the EU as it embarked on rapid economic modernisation after the peaceful transition to democracy following the death of dictator Francisco Franco. However the rapid debt-funded growth in housing and construction industries left the economy exposed to the global economic crisis of 2008/09. The government intervened to support the banking system and embarked on an austerity programme to reduce public debt and economic reforms to boost exports and reduce unemployment without resorting to a bailout.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-next="#next-spain" data-cycle-prev="#prev-spain" Monetary Fund; European Union. All data 2014 except contributions to EU budget which are for 2013.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="selected-info week-1-info" id="slovenia">\n<div class="close"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xclose.png.pagespeed.ic.jrZDHn-p-l.png"/></div>\n<div class="country"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/slovenia/xslovenia.png.pagespeed.ic.QE-Ho9fb2g.png"/><p><strong>Slovenia</strong><br>Capital : <strong>Ljubljana</strong><br>Joined EU: 2004</br></br></p></div>\n<div class="content">\n<p>Slovenia had always been the most prosperous region of the former Yugoslavia with high quality infrastructure and a well-educated work force. It has found the transition from a state-controlled economy to the free market easier than many in the region. Slovenia\xe2\x80\x99s main export and import partners are Germany, Italy and Austria. It fell into a deep recession following the European financial crisis in 2012 but escaped the need for a bailout thanks to tough austerity measures that sparked mass protests.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-next="#next-slovenia" data-cycle-prev="#prev-slovenia" data-cycle-slides="&gt; .item" data-cycle-swipe="true" data-cycle-timeout="0">\n<div class="item">\n<div class="item-group">\n<img class="img-responsive" Its exports make up more than 90% GDP, serving as the main driver of economic growth. It also built on a reputation of a relatively low-cost base, well-skilled labour force, reasonable tax rates, as well as its strategic geographic location in the heart of Central Europe to attract foreign direct investment. Industry makes up quarter of the economy.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-next="#next-slovakia" It is still dependent on agriculture and industry, which together make up a third of the economy. Exports are the engine of economic growth, led by trade with the EU, which accounts for roughly 70% of Romania trade. Its main export partners are Germany, Italy and France. It enjoyed the second fastest economic growth in the EU in 2013.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" However the financial crisis left it with massive public debt and in 2011 it became the third EU country after Greece and Ireland to ask international lenders for emergency assistance. The country exited the \xe2\x82\xac78bn programme in 2014 after achieving some success in cutting its deficit and restoring growth.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" Industry makes up almost a third of the economy and its main exports include machinery and transport equipment, and intermediate manufactured goods. The automotive sector is one of the largest industry sectors in Poland and is the 3rd largest manufacturer of buses in Europe. However agriculture still employs a large number of people, around 13% of the workforce, but produces less than 3% of the economy.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" Its main industrial activities are food processing, chemicals, petroleum refining, and electrical machinery. It has a sizeable but very contracted financial sector with just four banks dominating the sector. The sector suffered as a result of the global financial crisis and required billions of euros in government support.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" The economy is dependent on foreign trade, manufacturing, and tourism and produces only about 20% of its food needs. It weathered the financial crisis well and in 2014 was the strongest growing member of the eurozone, which it joined in 2008. Since 2014, the government has promoted public-private partnerships in the healthcare sector to establish Malta as a Mediterranean hub for medical tourism.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div All data 2014 except contributions to EU budget which are for 2013.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="selected-info week-1-info" id="luxembourg">\n<div class="close"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xclose.png.pagespeed.ic.jrZDHn-p-l.png"/></div>\n<div class="country"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/luxembourg/xluxembourg.png.pagespeed.ic.niAV1FSmje.png"/><p><strong>Luxembourg</strong><br>Capital : <strong>Luxembourg</strong><br>Joined EU: 1958</br></br></p></div>\n<div class="content">\n<p>The landlocked Grand Duchy of Luxembourg is both the richest country in the EU in terms of GDP per person and the second smallest in both physical size and population. It is a centre of financial and insurance activities, which make up some 27% of the economy. It also has a vibrant industrial sector, initially dominated by steel, that now include chemicals, machinery and equipment, rubber, and automotive components. Its main trading partners are its neighbours Germany, France and Belgium.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" Monetary Fund; European Union. All data 2014 except contributions to EU budget which are for 2013.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="selected-info week-1-info" id="lithuania">\n<div class="close"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xclose.png.pagespeed.ic.jrZDHn-p-l.png"/></div>\n<div class="country"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/lithuania/xlithuania.png.pagespeed.ic.a70ySSfRMD.png"/><p><strong>Lithuania</strong><br>Capital : <strong>Vilnius</strong><br>Joined EU: 2004</br></br></p></div>\n<div class="content">\n<p>Lithuania is the southernmost, most populous and largest of the three Baltic States (alongside Estonia and Latvia). It was hard hit by the global recession but was the only one of the three not to need a rescue package from the IMF and EU. However Lithuania\xe2\x80\x99s ongoing recovery from recession depends on export growth, which has been limited by the economic slowdowns in the EU and Russia, which is its main trading partner. It adopted the euro in 2015.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-next="#next-lithuania" It received assistance from the IMF, EU, and other international donors to defend the currency\'s peg to the euro, which it adopted in 2014. It was the eurozone\xe2\x80\x99s fastest growing economy in 2012 and 2013. Industry makes up a quarter of the economy with a special focus on timber and wood-processing, agriculture and food products.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" Monetary Fund; European Union. All data 2014 except contributions to EU budget which are for 2013.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="selected-info week-1-info" id="italy">\n<div class="close"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xclose.png.pagespeed.ic.jrZDHn-p-l.png"/></div>\n<div class="country"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/italy/xitaly.png.pagespeed.ic.QnpSLIKmWA.png"/><p><strong>Italy</strong><br>Capital : <strong>Rome</strong><br>Joined EU: 1958</br></br></p></div>\n<div class="content">\n<p>A founding member of the European Economic Community, the country can roughly be divided into a fully developed and industrial north and less-developed, agricultural south, where unemployment is higher. The economy is driven in part by the manufacture of high-quality famous-name consumer goods produced by small and medium-sized enterprises, many of them family-owned. Italy also has a sizeable informal economy, which may accounts for as much as 17% of GDP. It was hit by the global recession and had one of the highest levels of public debt in the EU but has avoided the need for a bailout.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" However the global financial crisis exposed excessive borrowing by its domestic property market and construction industry. It received a $92bn bailout from the IMF and EU and imposed tough austerity measures that allowed it to exit the bailout regime in 2013. Its main trading partners are fellow Anglophone western economies, the UK and the United States, which is a major investor in the country.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div Its main trading partners are Germany and Russia and industry makes up more than a quarter of the economy. After it was hit by the global recession in 2009 it obtained an IMF/EU/World Bank financial assistance package worth more than $25bn. It has since returned to strong growth supported by the industrial sector that makes up almost a third of the economy and which includes activities such as mining, metallurgy, and construction materials.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-next="#next-hungary" data-cycle-prev="#prev-hungary" Its economy is dependent on tourism, which makes up almost a fifth of the economy, and shopping. It is a beneficiary of EU aid. It went into recession in 2009 since when its economy has shrunk by 26% compared with pre-crisis levels while unemployment has soared to 26%.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-next="#next-greece" data-cycle-prev="#prev-greece" data-cycle-slides="&gt; .item" data-cycle-swipe="true" The economy is highly diversified with particular specialisms in chemicals, defence, financial services and power generation. Tourism is an important industry as more than 84 million foreign tourists visit every year making France the most visited country in the world and giving it the third largest income in the world from tourism.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-next="#next-france" data-cycle-prev="#prev-france" While services make up two-thirds of output it has a very competitive manufacturing industry, especially wood, metals, engineering, telecommunications, and electronics. Finland is home to Nokia, the telecoms business that sold its mobile phones arm to Microsoft in 2013.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" It now has the lowest level of gross public debt in the EU. Its main export partners are Sweden, Finland and Latvia. It adopted the euro in 2011. It boasts strengths in the electronics and telecommunications sectors.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" The country has the highest standard of living in the EU other than Luxembourg, an equitable distribution of income and generous welfare payments. Although it meets the criteria to join Economic and Monetary Union (EMU), Denmark has negotiated an opt-out with the EU and has retained the krone rather than adopt the euro.</p>\n</div>\n<div It was one of eight countries to join the EU in 2004. It has a relatively strong industrial base that makes up almost a third of the economy, a quarter of which is the auto sector. As a very open economy it was hard hit when Germany, its main trading partner, went into recession in 2008.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" The most important sectors are wholesale and retail trade, transport, accommodation and food services, which make up 21.2% of GDP in 2014, slightly more than industry (21.1%). Croatia will be a member of the European Exchange Rate Mechanism until it meets the criteria for joining the Economic and Monetary Union and adopts the euro as its currency.</p>\n</div>\n<div All data 2014 except contributions to EU budget which are for 2013.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="selected-info week-1-info" id="bulgaria">\n<div class="close"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xclose.png.pagespeed.ic.jrZDHn-p-l.png"/></div>\n<div class="country"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/bulgaria/xbulgaria.png.pagespeed.ic.QvQpKeKXe1.png"/><p><strong>Bulgaria</strong><br>Capital : <strong>Sofia</strong><br>Joined EU: 2007</br></br></p></div>\n<div class="content">\n<p>The poorest country in the EU as measured by GDP per person, Bulgaria has a reputation for organised crime and corruption that has deterred investors and hampered efforts to stimulate economic growth after decades of communist control. Industry is the largest economic activity making up almost 25% of growth. Its main trading partners are Germany and Italy.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-next="#next-bulgaria" Per<br>Person</br></br></p>\n</div>\n<div class="item-group">\n<img class="img-responsive" Monetary Fund; European Union. All data 2014 except contributions to EU budget which are for 2013.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="selected-info week-1-info" id="belgium">\n<div class="close"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xclose.png.pagespeed.ic.jrZDHn-p-l.png"/></div>\n<div class="country"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/belgium/xbelgium.png.pagespeed.ic.BFZWYIvVrQ.png"/><p><strong>Belgium</strong><br>Capital : <strong>Brussels</strong><br>Joined EU: 1958</br></br></p></div>\n<div class="content">\n<p>Belgium is home to many of the institutions of the European Union and was a founder member of its predecessor, the European Economic Community. It is a federal state divided into three regions: Dutch-speaking Flanders in the north; francophone Wallonia in the south; and Brussels where French and Dutch share official status. A member of the euro, around three-quarters of its trade is with other EU countries.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" Monetary Fund; European Union. All data 2014 except contributions to EU budget which are for 2013.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="selected-info week-1-info" id="austria">\n<div class="close"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xclose.png.pagespeed.ic.jrZDHn-p-l.png"/></div>\n<div class="country"><img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/austria/xaustria.png.pagespeed.ic.Eo7MRUX8er.png"/><p><strong>Austria</strong><br>Capital : <strong>Vienna</strong><br>Joined EU: 1995</br></br></p></div>\n<div class="content">\n<p>A landlocked mountainous country, Austria trades mainly with its near neighbours Germany Italy and Switzerland as well as with the US. It has a large service sector of which the most important subsectors are wholesale and retail, transport and food services. It has a relatively large industrial sector making up 22% of the economy, and a small but well developed agricultural sector. It is a member of the euro and its banks have exposure to central and eastern Europe and the ongoing crisis in Ukraine.</p>\n</div>\n<div class="slideshow">\n<div class="cycle-slideshow" data-cycle-fx="fade" data-cycle-log="false" data-cycle-next="#next-austria" data-cycle-prev="#prev-austria" data-cycle-slides="&gt; .item" data-cycle-swipe="true" data-cycle-timeout="0">\n<div Per<br>Person</br></br></p>\n</div>\n<div class="item-group">\n<img class="img-responsive"
