Monetary Fund; European Union. All data 2014 except contributions to EU budget which are for 2013.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="prev-week-content">\n<h3 style="padding-top:20px;">Speed of monetary union has left capital markets in slow lane</h3>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="col-sm-8">\n<div class="main-content">\n<div class="content-pic">\n<img alt="Euro_650x300" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" data-lazy-src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/10/Euro_650x300.jpg" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/lazy-load/images/1x1.trans.gif"/><noscript><img alt="Euro_650x300" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/10/xEuro_650x300.jpg.pagespeed.ic.JVIzacIhZp.jpg"/></noscript> <p></p>\n<p></p>\n</div>\n<div class="content" id="main-content">\n<p><strong>As the euro\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98birthday\xe2\x80\x99 nears, Phil Thornton looks at the benign neglect that is only now being addressed.</strong></p>\n<p>January 1, 2016 will mark the 15th anniversary of the launch of euro notes and coins across what was then the 11-member single currency zone. For many Europeans, that date is the birthday of the euro.</p>\n<p>Yet that event, momentous though it was, was only the final step in an intellectual and political journey that can be traced back to 1970 when then Luxembourg prime minister, Pierre Werner, published a major report on how economic and monetary union (EMU) could be achieved by 1980.</p>\n<p>However his report, which proposed fixing national currencies ahead of the possible creation of a single currency, was overshadowed by the collapse of the Bretton Woods exchange rate system and the surge in inflation across Europe.</p>\n<p>It was not until 1988 that a meeting chaired by the then president of the European Commission, Jacques Delors, defined the objectives of monetary union as not just replacing national currencies with a single unit but also completing the liberalisation of capital movements and full integration of financial markets.</p>\n<p>But while monetary union went full speed ahead with the establishment of the European Central Bank and the launch of the currency, the creation of a capital markets union \xe2\x80\x94 along with the development of fiscal and political unions \xe2\x80\x94 were left in the slow lane.<br/>\nHolger Schmieding, chief economist at the German bank Berenberg and a long standing observer of European integration, said that political leaders had assumed that a single currency would be a stepping stone on the journey to closer union.</p>\n<p>\xe2\x80\x9cThey believed that once you had a single monetary area, capital markets integration would happen on its own,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cNot much thought was given as to who would be the lender of last resort, or whether diverging national frameworks for capital market regulation would lead to problems in a financial crisis.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p>That policy of benign neglect suited the creators of the eurozone very well during the 1980s, 1990s and 2000s. The eurozone was enjoying stronger growth, more stable housing markets, lower inflation and fewer fiscal problems than the UK.</p>\n<p>Even before the official creation of the single currency there had been substantial capital integration within the eurozone, with large inflows of capital and dramatic convergence of bond yields as investors started to price out different levels of risk between member states.<br/>\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith rapid integration happening, the idea of needing to think about extra regulations to get more integration did not seem an urgent issue,\xe2\x80\x9d said Schmieding. In a perfect world, he added, the fathers of the European project should have gone further at the time of the Delors report and created a lender of last resort for failing banks.</p>\n<p>As Jean-Claude Juncker, the current president of the European Commission, said in a major report in June 2014, economic and monetary union was \xe2\x80\x9clike a house that was built over decades but only partially finished\xe2\x80\x9d.</p>\n<p>The Commission itself conceded in September 2015, on the launch of its recent capital markets union initiative, that Europe\xe2\x80\x99s capital markets remain \xe2\x80\x9cfragmented along national lines\xe2\x80\x9d.</p>\n<p>Graham Bishop, a consultant on EU integration, said the idea of creating a capital markets union does go back to the foundations of the EU. \xe2\x80\x9cMore specifically, the 1992 single market programme included all the initial pieces of legislation that are now being modernised and upgraded.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nHe added that the original programme was conceived and driven by Lord Cockfield, Margaret Thatcher\xe2\x80\x99s appointee to the European Commission. Therefore it was appropriate that the European commissioner now in charge of overseeing the next stage of the union was Lord Hill, appointed by the current Conservative prime minister David Cameron.</p>\n<p>Mr Schmieding said he was encouraged by the fact that policymakers were learning from the crisis. \xe2\x80\x9cAll in all, the proposals go very much in the right direction and basically fix the gaps in the architecture of the eurozone.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n</div>\n<div class="quote hidden-xs">\n<img src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/themes/html5blank-stable/img/xquotes.jpg.pagespeed.ic.0Ijl46bHM-.jpg"/>\n<p>The objectives of monetary union are not just replacing national currencies with a single unit but also completing the liberalisation of capital movements and full integration of financial markets</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="col-sm-4">\n<div class="bg-blue gt-article">\n<h5 style="margin:0;">Advertisement Feature</h5>\n<div class="gt-title">\n<h3>Comment &amp; Analysis</h3>\n<p>Huw Pill</p>\n<p>Chief European Economist, Goldman Sachs</p>\n<img class="img-responsive" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Huw-Pill.jpg"/>\n<p><strong>Goldman Sachs</strong></p>\n</div>\n<div class="gt-linebreak"></div>\n<p>The EU\xe2\x80\x99s financial markets have to work as one if the euro is to flourish. The advent of the single currency promised to be a catalyst for the creation of continent-wide capital markets in Europe, which would, in turn, support economic growth and job creation. But the sustainability of monetary union rested on having an integrated, Europe-wide financial sector that was able to put the single monetary policy into operation throughout the euro area. The first decade of monetary union after 1999 illustrated how well these systems can work together. The absence of currency risks within the euro area created more competitive financial markets and facilitated larger cross-border flows of capital. Credit became more available and borrowing costs fell. As a result, companies were able to invest more than before, enhancing growth prospects. Cross-border capital flows also led to a more deeply integrated financial sector which, in parallel, made it possible for monetary policy to be efficiently passed on to businesses and households, allowing the European Central Bank (ECB) to maintain an environment of broadly stable prices and resilient demand. The second decade has proved rather less benign. The global financial crisis disrupted the supply of credit and led to the capital markets once again fragmenting along national lines, interfering with the transmission of monetary policy. Across Europe, macroeconomic stability was put at risk. Moreover, the ECB was unable to provide support to those countries most in need, creating political as well as economic strains that put the future of the euro in question. The threat of a euro break-up exacerbated the fragmentation, creating a vicious downward spiral that had a strong destabilising effect.<br/>\r\nEstablishing a true single market for capital entails addressing the institutional shortcomings that underlie the divisions between financial markets. There are efforts at the moment to build a banking union and a capital market union so that the institutions across the EU would operate to common rules, and these are important steps. Without a more integrated and better functioning financial sector, the workability of monetary union will continue to be in question.</p>\n<p class="gt-terms">This content was produced by the Advertising Department of The Times in collaboration with Goldman Sachs. The news and editorial staffs of The Times had no role in its preparation.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="share">\n<div class="a2a_kit a2a_kit_size_32 src="//static.addtoany.com/menu/page.js"></script>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<button class="btn readmore">Read More</button>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<!-- end of the loop -->\n<div class="tracked_article bg-white" id="which-european-nations-are-leading-the-way-in-sme-funding">\n<div class="container">\n<div class="content-border">\n<div class="tab">\n                                        MORE FROM THE TIMES\n                                    </div>\n<h3>Which European nations are leading the way in SME funding?</h3>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="related-articles">\n<div class="col-sm-10 col-sm-offset-1">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="sub-article-excerpt">\n<div class="col-sm-7">\n<img alt="Map2" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" data-lazy-src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Map2.jpg" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/lazy-load/images/1x1.trans.gif"/><noscript><img alt="Map2" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Map2.jpg"/></noscript> </div>\n<div class="col-sm-5">\n<p><strong>You don\xe2\x80\x99t have to be a major corporation to make a difference on the European stage, but you may have to fight a little harder to find the funding you need.</strong> Small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) \xe2\x80\x93 firms with fewer than 250 employees and less than 50 million euros in annual turnover \xe2\x80\x93 make up a significant part of the EU\xe2\x80\x99s economy: around 99 per\xe2\x80\xa6</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="sub-article-content">\n<div class="col-sm-12">\n<img alt="Map2" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" data-lazy-src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Map2.jpg" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/lazy-load/images/1x1.trans.gif"/><noscript><img alt="Map2" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Map2.jpg"/></noscript> <p><p><strong>You don\xe2\x80\x99t have to be a major corporation to make a difference on the European stage, but you may have to fight a little harder to find the funding you need.</strong><br/>\nSmall and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) \xe2\x80\x93 firms with fewer than 250 employees and less than 50 million euros in annual turnover \xe2\x80\x93 make up a significant part of the EU\xe2\x80\x99s economy: around 99 per cent of all non-financial companies and two thirds of all jobs.<br/>\nLike all businesses, SMEs often rely on injections of capital, to fund new projects or growth. Chris Lowe, a corporate finance specialist at the global accountancy firm EY, says: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s tough out there for SMEs because they are very entrepreneurially driven and that doesn\xe2\x80\x99t make the best kind of prospect for banks, which want a swift return on their investments.\xe2\x80\x9d To add to the challenge for SMEs seeking funding, the structure of the EU means that they face very different financing options in each country.<br/>\nVarious types of funding are used in different nations as each has its own financing costs, interest rates and credit conditions, as well as distinct monetary policies and tax frameworks, with those in southern Europe often offering the most challenging conditions for SMEs. At the same time, there is less supervision and regulation in newer EU states, which can make investment there less attractive for equity crowdfunding.<br/>\nOf the SME financing in the EU, it is generally accepted that about 80% comes from banks and 20 per cent from equity. This is a very different environment from the US, where there is a different attitude to risk \xe2\x80\x93 in 2014, for example, \xc2\xa320 billion was raised through venture capital there, compared with \xc2\xa33.5 billion in the EU.<br/>\nThe EU figure masks variation across regions. Rick Watson, the head of capital markets at the Association for Financial Markets in Europe \xe2\x80\x93 which recently released a special report on SME funding issues across the EU \xe2\x80\x93 says: \xe2\x80\x9cWhen the financial crisis hit, funding opportunities changed between countries. For instance, SMEs in southern Europe, where perhaps the recession lasted longer because of economic sluggishness, found it particularly tough to raise capital.<br/>\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnce bank after bank has said no, an SME might just stop trying, as it cannot access a developed capital network like the one in the US \xe2\x80\x93 where governments, universities, banks and individual investors work together. That fragmentation is a piece of the puzzle that EU authorities need to solve.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nAlthough loans are still very important in most countries, many other models of financing are now available \xe2\x80\x93 such as private equity, venture capital, business angels and crowdfunding \xe2\x80\x93 but uptake of these and other innovative approaches varies.<br/>\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe UK has led the way in more adventurous forms of SME capital investment,\xe2\x80\x9d Watson says, \xe2\x80\x9csuch as peer-to-peer, venture capital and crowdfunding. Other countries, such as those in Scandinavia, and especially Sweden, saw that becoming a \xe2\x80\x98specialist\xe2\x80\x99 in a certain type of tech SME, would lead to greater chances of outside investment. And in Germany, the solution has been collaborative, with the funding community working together impressively to create greater access to capital.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nWestern Europe is far more open to the use of so-called non-banks, including loan funds, crowdfunding platforms, leasing companies, insurers, invoice finance providers, community finance organisations and large corporates. This is in part because of stronger internal economies but also because SMEs represent a less risky investment, as they can often show a good track record. This open-mindedness has led to individual states specialising in certain types of financing \xe2\x80\x93 Luxembourg and Ireland, for instance, lead the way in investment funds, and the UK is ahead of the game with private equity.<br/>\nHowever, differences in lending rates can mean SMEs in some nations can\xe2\x80\x99t afford some of the newer loan systems. For instance, a typical crowdfunding lending rate in the UK is about 4 per cent \xe2\x80\x93 in Spain it can be three times that.<br/>\nThe use of listed shares is common in western Europe whereas debt securities are generally unavailable to firms in the least developed EU countries. In addition, businesses in southern Europe (especially those owned and run by families) are hugely reluctant to cede control to capital markets rather than banks. In 2013, for instance, Italian SMEs received \xc2\xa3163 billion in bank loans compared to just \xc2\xa3700 million in private equity.<br/>\nMeanwhile, SMEs in some countries (France, Germany, Spain, Italy and the UK) have benefited from funding and advice issued by public investment banks owned by the state, a facility not shared in other European states.<br/>\nEquity finance is more limited too \xe2\x80\x93 the EU\xe2\x80\x99s stockmarkets were worth 64.5 per cent of GDP in 2013 \xe2\x80\x93 half of America\xe2\x80\x99s 138 per cent. And market capitalisations vary across the EU: in the same period Britain\xe2\x80\x99s was more than 120 per cent of GDP compared with just 35 per cent for Italy \xe2\x80\x93 another reason for the country\xe2\x80\x99s over-reliance on banks.<br/>\nAll of this diversity creates challenges for those trying to simplify life for SMEs. The European Commission\xe2\x80\x99s new \xc2\xa3221 billion European Fund for Strategic Investments, which aims to plug the gap in funding for SMEs, will go some way to sparking their growth, but some commentators identify equity finance as the missing factor.<br/>\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is not a message that SMEs want to hear \xe2\x80\x93 especially on the continent \xe2\x80\x93 but ultimately it\xe2\x80\x99s equity finance that\xe2\x80\x99s going to help a business,\xe2\x80\x9d Lowe says. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a terrible muddle out there at the moment and pan-European authorities need to co-ordinate their investment strategies much better and encourage more equity financing \xe2\x80\x93 that way there will be a better path to stability for SMEs.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="share">\n<div class="a2a_kit a2a_kit_size_32 a2a_default_style" data-a2a-title="Which European nations are leading the way in SME funding?" data-a2a-url="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/week1/which-european-nations-are-leading-the-way-in-sme-funding/">\n<a class="a2a_button_facebook"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_twitter"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_google_plus"></a>\n<a class="a2a_dd" href="https://www.addtoany.com/share"></a>\n</div>\n<script async="" src="//static.addtoany.com/menu/page.js"></script>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p style="color:#000;font-size:14px;margin-top:15px;"></p>\n</div>\n<button class="btn readmore">Read More</button>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="tracked_article bg-white" id="how-business-schools-are-helping-to-shape-europes-financial-future">\n<div class="container">\n<div class="content-border">\n<div class="tab">\n                                        MORE FROM THE TIMES\n                                    </div>\n<h3>How business schools are helping to shape Europe\xe2\x80\x99s financial future</h3>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="related-articles">\n<div class="col-sm-10 col-sm-offset-1">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="sub-article-excerpt">\n<div class="col-sm-7">\n<img alt="Map1" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" data-lazy-src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Map1.jpg" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/lazy-load/images/1x1.trans.gif"/><noscript><img alt="Map1" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Map1.jpg"/></noscript> </div>\n<div class="col-sm-5">\n<p><strong>Today\xe2\x80\x99s MBA and finance graduates are tomorrow\xe2\x80\x99s investment bankers \xe2\x80\x93 and, quite possibly, the industry\xe2\x80\x99s policymakers and regulators.</strong> This means that business schools have a substantial role to play in shaping the future of Europe\xe2\x80\x99s capital markets, according to Colin Mayer, the Peter Moores professor of management at Oxford\xe2\x80\x99s Sa\xc3\xafd Business School. \xe2\x80\x9cFinance is central to a business school education,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the way\xe2\x80\xa6</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="sub-article-content">\n<div class="col-sm-12">\n<img alt="Map1" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" data-lazy-src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Map1.jpg" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/plugins/lazy-load/images/1x1.trans.gif"/><noscript><img alt="Map1" class="img-responsive wp-post-image" src="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Map1.jpg"/></noscript> <p><p><strong>Today\xe2\x80\x99s MBA and finance graduates are tomorrow\xe2\x80\x99s investment bankers \xe2\x80\x93 and, quite possibly, the industry\xe2\x80\x99s policymakers and regulators.</strong> This means that business schools have a substantial role to play in shaping the future of Europe\xe2\x80\x99s capital markets, according to Colin Mayer, the Peter Moores professor of management at Oxford\xe2\x80\x99s Sa\xc3\xafd Business School. \xe2\x80\x9cFinance is central to a business school education,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the way in which business schools frame discussions about capital markets will have a significant influence on how future leaders view them.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nStefano Caselli, professor of banking and finance at Bocconi University in Milan, agrees, but adds that business schools do not work in isolation; practising bankers are also part of the picture. \xe2\x80\x9cThey give speeches and work with professors to design courses,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is synergy\xe2\x80\xa6 business schools both lead and follow. You have to know what investment banks are doing in markets, but on the other hand business schools also provide views and guidelines that, in a sense, try to imagine the future. And in many cases those views become models.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nBusiness schools are focusing their attention on the efficiency of capital markets and the benefits to be gained from market integration and banking union across Europe, says Mayer. \xe2\x80\x9cA second element is the role of banking and the funding of investment in Europe, particularly the financing of small and medium sized businesses.\xe2\x80\x9d Regulation and its effects on European financial markets is a third critical strand, while the role of governance and ownership in equity markets is the final part of the foundation.<br/>\nAs a result Mayer expects to see increasing pressure for Europe to take a strong stance on capital market integration and banking union. \xe2\x80\x9cFinancial markets in Europe will be expected to play a more effective role in terms of financing capital investment. There is concern that investment in Europe has been held back because of a failure of banking systems in particular, but also capital markets more generally, to provide the depth of funding that is needed and is observed in, for example, the US.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nTheo Vermaelen, a professor of finance at Insead, in Fontainebleau, is less sure about the impact that he and his academic peers will have on the markets\xe2\x80\x99 futures. \xe2\x80\x9cWe all like to think that we have a huge impact as professors, but it is probably limited,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. \xe2\x80\x9cEducation can make a difference but it is a long process. The people we teach are young and not very powerful. We have to hope that what we teach them, they remember for 20 years.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nOne area where he has seen academic influence at work is in relation to the US markets. Most high-profile academics at European schools have studied and undertaken significant research in the US and are likely to be more familiar with US markets than the full spread of European market, says Vermaelen. Another factor is that it is easier to study US market data, as it is more complete and accessible; US-focused research also makes it easier for professors to get papers published in prestigious American journals.<br/>\nAs a result, market innovations from the States end up on European business school curriculums, he says; this may go on to shape the tools and techniques used here. For example, he teaches students about share buybacks \xe2\x80\x93 popular in the US in the wake of the financial crisis \xe2\x80\x93 although some students question why they are learning about an area that has not caught on here. \xe2\x80\x9cI say, \xe2\x80\x98It is your role to be business leaders of the future, to make sure that these things get implemented\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d says Vermaelen. \xe2\x80\x9cI predict that what happens in America will come to Europe, with some delay.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nBusiness schools\xe2\x80\x99 relationship with capital markets reflects the cyclical nature of financial markets themselves, says Caselli. \xe2\x80\x9cTwenty years ago professors aimed to create theories and models to make new tools to run businesses, such as derivatives. Now theories and models are designed to support regulators. In ten or twenty years that will change\xe2\x80\xa6 it is the story of financial markets since the beginning.\xe2\x80\x9d<br/>\nMayer adds: \xe2\x80\x9cIn response to the financial crisis there has been more emphasis on macro issues, on the protection of the financial system as a whole. That is something I believe will accelerate over the next few years. There will be attempts to provide better coordination across different regulatory bodies across European capital markets.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n<p><strong>The student\xe2\x80\x99s view: I need to understand it all</strong><br/>\nAlexander Reneby, 28, thinks that the future of European capital markets will be shaped by graduates with a strong grasp of both the quantitative and business aspects of the markets. He chose his course \xe2\x80\x93 a masters in finance taught at Lund University, in Sweden, and Emlyon, in Lyon \xe2\x80\x93 because it offered both elements. \xe2\x80\x9cThe financial crisis showed that there were a lot of people in sales selling things that they did not fully understand, and mathematicians building models that they did not know what they were for,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. \xe2\x80\x9cI think that created a lot of problems. People have to have a solid foundation of knowledge in both parts even if they then specialise. I think that students have a responsibility to look for business schools that teach both theory and practice.\xe2\x80\x9d</p>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="share">\n<div class="a2a_kit a2a_kit_size_32 a2a_default_style" data-a2a-title="How business schools are helping to shape Europe\xe2\x80\x99s financial future" data-a2a-url="http://www.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk/week1/how-business-schools-are-helping-to-shape-europes-financial-future/">\n<a class="a2a_button_facebook"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_twitter"></a>\n<a class="a2a_button_google_plus"></a>\n<a class="a2a_dd" href="https://www.addtoany.com/share"></a>\n</div>\n<script async="" src="//static.addtoany.com/menu/page.js"></script>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p style="color:#000;font-size:14px;margin-top:15px;"></p>\n</div>\n<button class="btn readmore">Read More</button>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="bg-blue" id="encouraging-growth-the-role-of-the-european-banking-union">\n<div class="container">\n<div class="content-border">\n<div class="tab">\n                                        VIDEO\n                                    </div>\n<h3 class="tracked_port">Encouraging Growth: The Role of the European Banking Union</h3>\n<div class="row">\n<div class="related-articles">\n<div class="col-sm-10 col-sm-offset-1">\n<div class="row">\n<div class="sub-article-excerpt">\n<div class="col-sm-7">\n<div class="fluid-width-video-wrapper" style="min-height:260px;">\n<!-- Featured Video Plus v2.2.2 -->\n<div class="featured-video-plus post-thumbnail fvp-responsive fvp-youtube fvp-center"><iframe allowfullscreen="" frameborder="0" height="506" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/sIm75VS4JOM?autoplay&amp;list=PLIyiGQywEp67orByG2yucb66N3FtgYRnt&amp;origin=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.understandingcapitalmarkets.co.uk" width="900"></iframe></div>\n<img alt="Featured Video Play Icon" class="fvp-onload" onload="(function() {(\'initFeaturedVideoPlus\' in this) &amp;&amp;
